Chapter XV.
On the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in certain individuals such that the eyelid is adhered above to the sclera, and it is prohibited from its ease of movement and natural functions, then you should introduce a probe below the eyelid, and lift it up with it, or extend it with forceps.
And then you should cut the adhesion with a thin blade, which is not sharp enough to cause pain from the cutting, holding it blunt so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and remove the tissue without scraping the wound with its sharpness.
And let your cutting be such that it gently removes that adhesion, allowing the eyelid to return to its natural form, and ensuring that all of the adhesion is healed.
And then you should put salt water on the wound with which you wash it; or distill in the juice of rosemary dissolved in water.
And then separate between the eyelid and the wound with a cloth, and apply on the wound linen infused in egg white, and on the third day use healing substances until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On cutting of nails and of flesh growth near the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two kinds, either it is nervous, and resembles hard dry skin; or it is non-nervous, resembling frozen white moisture, which when touched by iron, can be forcibly removed without any residue being left behind.
And both kinds do not begin except at the larger tear duct, gradually covering the eye and prohibiting light and the movement of the eyelid.
But the method of working in cutting it is that the patient places their head in your lap, and then opens their eyes, and you lift the eyelid with your hand, and then collect the nail by slightly doubling it back and extend it to the parts above. Chapter XVII.
On the incision of gangrenous tissue and what is born of tight flesh in the liquid.

On the reduction of the prominence of the belly.

Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of hernias.
This illness that is called hernia is not the one referred to as an inflammation that descends like water but is otherwise different.

And if it does not descend below, know that it is water.

Once it is expelled, then you should apply water that has been boiled and in which you have mixed honey or water in which fenugreek and honey have already been boiled.
And then treat it with the other methods until it is healed.

Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of flesh wounds on the nose.
When on the nose there arise various flesh growths that are attached, some of which have causes similar to a scorpion made of soft words, or to a sea polyp; and this illness is called polypus because it resembles such a sea creature. But if you prefer a drying cure, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument for making enemas, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nose:
And make this of silver or ivory, which should resemble a small spoon covered, like a small cup, with the opening similarly shaped.
And if you wish, make a tube with a joint like a cane, and the tube of the instrument for making enemas should be flat and open; to which attach a handle at the back, so that you can hold it when heating in oil or whatever you want from juices and suctionable causes.
Chapter XXV.
About thorns in the nostrils.
Often thorns become stuck in the extremities of the nose, and after being extracted, they are bandaged and ointment is applied for several days during which one must be careful about proper care.
And for this reason, you should incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and heal all of it. And then pause on the place where you cauterized or applied burning medicine, which was there before it.
But if the incision is delayed until it has been bandaged, now consider whether it is hardened, tough, dark in color, and with little sensation; then do not touch it with iron because it is a chronic abscess. For often, when you incise this abscess, great harm will come to the one who suffers from it.
And if the abscess is easily touched and not dark in color, and when incision is possible throughout it, then evacuate the patient's illness and incise it without fear, and treat the area with something that dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
On to sew up the nose and lips and ear when the continuity has been dissolved by plague or similar.
Know that when dissolution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, few will undertake such an operation except in some men.
Therefore, when dealing with any cause of these conditions, you should consider whether it is a fresh wound or involves the loss of flesh and blood. Then sew together the two sides of the wound, and subsequently treat what lies within so that it may heal.
And if the edges of the continuity dissolution are already separated and each side is clean, then you should scrape each part of its apparent edge until blood comes out. And then sew the two edges together with stitches and strengthen them. Over these, apply powder of dragon's blood and sprinkle it, and place over the powder a piece of mastic plaster or another consolidating plaster. Let this remain for two days or three, and then remove it and change the plaster until the pus has stopped, and subsequently treat internally so that it may heal.
But the method of sewing is that you may join the continuity dissolution either with needles as we have recommended for sewing the abdomen, or you may join it with thread as you see fit.
Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of those causes that appear in the two edges in their internal parts.
In many cases, men develop hard swellings in the partitions inside their lips, resembling horse fat, and some of them are smaller while others are larger.
Then cut the lips and divide each of those hardnesses from all sides, and after the incision, fill the place with finely chopped wool so that the blood is constricted, and then wash it with something sour and treat the area with water that has astringent properties until it heals.
Chapter XXVIII.
About incising the flesh attached to the gums.
Often there is flesh attached to the gum, which the ancients called "albulus." Then you should suspend that with a thread or whatever you have and incise it near its base, and allow the pus or blood to flow.
And then apply finely chopped wool or other drying powders to the area.
And if that flesh returns, incise it again and cauterize it, for after the cauterization it will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
About extracting teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth in the inner and outer parts, and between the gums to which they are attached, there are rough spots, tartar deposits, and sometimes darkened areas that turn yellow or green, causing corruption to the gum and making the teeth tartar-covered.
Therefore, make the patient sit between your legs and place his head in your lap, and rasp the teeth in which appear such tartar deposits and causes similar to sand until there remains none of them, and do this for the blackness and yellowness and for the others until they are cleansed.
Therefore, if it is still in that of the beginning of the treatment, it is good; and otherwise, then you repeat the treatment on those on another day and on the second and on the third until it reaches its final stage as you wish.
And know that the teeth require various tools of different forms and shapes according to how they are prepared for your operation. And because the razors with which the tooth is cut from inside are different from those used to rasp between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of razors you should prepare as you will see shortly:
Chapter XXX.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
When you treat the tooth of its pain with all diligence and delay in its entire extraction, for there is no reason that suffices to replace it when it is extracted, since it is a substance sublime wherein lies the necessity of its extraction.
But when the patient is compelled to have that tooth extracted, you must firmly be certain which is the painful tooth, for sometimes the pain deceives the patient and he thinks it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one but shortly afterward the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is removed, as we have already seen through the experience of practitioners.
Thus when you are certain which is the painful tooth, and once you know well, then it is necessary that you incise around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the tooth and gums entirely, and then pull it out with your fingers or with fine forceps gradually little by little until you have extracted it.
And then use strong pliers for good leverage or force, and place the head of the patient between your knees so that he does not move, and then extract the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause greater pain to the patient.
And so if it is not loose, then take one of those instruments and place it around the tooth from all sides with ease and endeavor to remove it as was done initially.
But if the tooth is fractured or decayed, then you should wrap that fracture with cloth and secure it at the tip with a thin thread so that it does not break during your constriction on it with pliers.
And you must operate extremely carefully when incising around the entire gum area and guard against using too much strength so that you do not break it or leave part of it behind, and return to the patient with care lest the subsequent pain be worse than the initial discomfort.
And be careful that you do not act as some ignorant barbers do in their hastiness and audacity regarding the extraction of teeth; for they often fail to administer what we have recommended. Many times this leads to great harm to sick people, making it much more difficult to remove them later, or leaving the roots of the teeth exposed, or causing you to extract them along with some bones of the jaw, as many instances have testified.
Before the removal of the tooth, let the mouth be washed with hot water or vinegar diluted with salt.
And if blood flows because of this, just as you would do in similar cases, then now take some cerement and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that does not suffice, apply a cautery.
The form of the forceps and holding them delicately with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be long, short-handed, and thick, so that they do not bend when you are working with them.
And they should be like pieces of thick handle so that when you grasp them, they are not bent, their extremities should be short.
And they should be made of Indian iron, with straight extremities, and at the ends of those there should be teeth, some of which enter into others and grasp by strong constriction, and sometimes the extremities of those should have the shape of a bird's beak called a ciconia, which is a kite, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
On the removal of teeth and the extraction of the bones of the locked jaw.
When you need to remove a tooth that is trapped due to some cause, then it is necessary for you to place a poultice on the area with boiled substances for one day or two until the site becomes softened; and afterwards use such tools or forceps with ends shaped like the beak of a bird called ciconia or guanta, the form of the forceps:
Their extremities should be treated inside just as the limb or the forehead.
And if he does not obey you when using these forceps or tongs, then you should cut above this ridge and remove all the flesh, and insert the instrument called atali, which is a small lever whose form is as follows:
And it should have a short thick part about an inch long, and it should not be hollow so that it does not rattle.
Then if by this the ridge is properly exposed, it is good; and if not, assist with these other instruments of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is a triangular end in which there should be some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it should have a triangular and finer end.
And now assist with this instrument which has two prongs, of which this is the form as follows: and with other instruments which will be described for tooth scraping; because this instrument is useful, and this is its form:
with a triangular end that is curved or short, which should have some thickness, and it should not be hollow so that it does not rattle.
And know that the instruments for tooth extraction are similar and likewise resemble other instruments.
But the skilled and subtle artisan finds for himself according to what he understands from the operation and various diseases; for there are diseases about which the ancients did not say or leave a proper instrument, due to their varied forms.
If the bone of the jaw or any other bones in the mouth is fractured, or if it hurts, then at that time place the dressing on it as appropriate for the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of the roots of teeth, and assist with gestures of this form:
And it should be removed little by little, and should be held in the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until all of the bone is evacuated; and then restore the place with appropriate medicines.
If there is a small ulcer in the bone, then remove that of that ulcer and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of teeth that protrude beyond the others.
When the teeth that protrude do not follow their natural course and particularly when they affect women, especially those of venerable age; therefore, it is necessary for you to consider: if a tooth is close to another tooth and there is no possibility of filing or covering it, then extract it; but if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument whose form is as follows; it resembles the tusk of a boar, and is made of Indian steel with an extremely sharp point.
And your incision should be such that when you cut it, it does not take more than a few days due to the hardness of the tooth, and it should not become inflamed among the other teeth.
And if it is a protruding tooth that can be filed down, then file it with an Indian steel file whose form is as follows:
And let it be of Indian iron and the edge perfectly; and let it be of small points as with a file, with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over several days with ease, so that it may not become another tooth or gum. And apply strips to it at the end, and cut that with razors.
And if it is a tooth of which part is broken and you use the tongue to shape it, now you must file that inside until the roughness of that fracture is smoothed and filled so that the tongue does not wear it down nor the word be corrupted.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver filling.
When it happens that the front teeth are knocked out or loosened by impact or fall, and the patient cannot chew on them properly, even after you have treated them with astringent medicines and your treatment has not been effective, now in order to prevent them from falling out, fix those that need it with gold filling and not silver, because silver changes color and quickly becomes noticeable, whereas gold never changes.
And let the filling be in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can enter between the teeth; and the shape of the filling is that you take the filling and place twice as much between two healthy teeth, and then extend the filling between one or more broken ones, until it reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then continue the treatment at the part where you began, and strengthen your work with ease and skill so that it is not damaged.
And so let your thread be such that you can pull out the filings of the teeth without evasion.
And then cut off the excess part of the thread using forceps or scissors, and attach it in each place with skill, and convert and hide them between the healthy tooth and the affected tooth, so that it does not cause injury to the tongue. And then make that as narrow as a Roman coin.
But if it is loosened or broken, pull that or those out during the entire time and replace them with another thread.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the correction of two healthy teeth and of two affected teeth, as you see them tied:
And when it happens that you return one tooth or two near their proper places, and they grow back as we have recommended, and they remain healthy.
And only a skilled artisan and wise person can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cattle bone is used for some reason, and it is shaped like a tooth, and placed in the location where the tooth is missing, and it is attached as we have said, and remains and serves there for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the cutting of the bandage that goes around the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament that goes under the tongue sometimes is present from birth, or it is accidental and occurs due to an injury that has already healed.
And the operation is that of opening the mouth of the patient, and placing his head in such a way; and raise his tongue, and then incise that band of nerves according to its breadth as far as it is loosened from its attachment.
And if there is any hardness or swelling in that area, and this has occurred due to the consolidation of an illness, then you should cut through it lengthwise until you have severed the band so that the swelling may be dissolved.
And be careful that you do not make a cut in the surface of the flesh and thereby sever an artery, causing a flow of blood.
And then wash the remnants of the incision with salt water or vinegar water or plain water; and afterwards leave the tongue hanging loose; this is done so that the patient will hang their tongue out each night to prevent it from healing in an improper manner.
But if bleeding occurs, apply over the area powdered vitriol; but if the blood flows continuously, then cauterize the area with a hot iron, which is appropriate for this situation. And then proceed with the treatment using other methods until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
On the extraction of a cyst beneath the tongue.
At times, a cyst appears beneath the tongue, resembling a small swelling that prevents the tongue from its natural movement and can grow large enough to fill the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the mouth of the patient facing towards the light and examine the cyst.
And if you see it of dark brown or black color and hard, in which the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch that which is gangrenous.
And if it inclines towards a whiteness, in which there is humidity, then you place the incision in that area, and dissect it with a fine scalpel, and cut it from all parts; and if blood flows during the hour of your operation, then pause for three breaths, which is vitriol, until the blood congeals.
And then return to your work once you have properly treated it, after washing it with vinegar and salt; and treat the area with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter XXXVI.
On the extraction of abscesses from both ears and those that are in the throat and other abscesses.
When I refer to the area in the throat that is glandular and assembled somewhat together, which appears outside and are called ears, when you treat them with what we have said in the division and they do not heal, then consider whether the abscess is hard, dark brown in color, with little sensation; then do not touch that part with an instrument.
And if it is of red color and the root is thick, then do not touch that part with an instrument due to the flow of blood; instead, let that remain until it has ripened, and then either you will extract it or it will burst on its own.
And if it is of white color, soft, and the root of it is delicate, then that should be dissected.
In the operation that you consider, before your operation, see if the abscess from the cautery has healed completely or if it has diminished in any way; and then have the patient lie facing the sun, with their head turned to the side, and open the mouth of the patient, and the servant takes between his hands the tongue of the patient down with an instrument of noble form, which is this shape:
And it should be made of silver or iron, and it should be fine like a knife.
When you press with that the tongue and the abscess is shown to you, then take some (material) and place it under the affected area, then pull it outwards as far as you can. Take another piece of cloth for some reason and then cut it with the instrument whose shape is as follows:
Take another piece of cloth for some reason and then cut it with the instrument whose shape is as follows: and it resembles a pair of forceps, but the ends are curved, so that the opening of each is aligned with the other, sharp, made of iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a scalpel whose shape is as follows: It is sharp on one side and not sharp on the other side.
And after you have made one incision, make the other in the same manner of cutting, and then cleanse the patient's wound with fresh water.
But if there is bleeding, cleanse the wound with water infused with lemon peels or myrtle leaves and similar astringent substances until the flow of blood is stopped, and then treat it until it heals.
And in some cases we incise in the neck other abscesses or those two ears, and we incise those as is said in the incision of two melons equally.
And I had already treated a woman with an abscess which was born in her throat, the color of which tended to be dark, and it had little sensation, and it had closed her throat, and the woman breathed very narrowly, and she had been prohibited from eating and drinking water, and she would have reached death if it had not been for a day or two.
And the abscess had spread so much that it reached two sides and emerged through two places.
Then I decided to operate and inserted a probe into one of the openings, and pulled it out, and much matter came out from there, and then I incised that which included the other opening, and then I closed its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and then I placed the probe in that abscess.
And then I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Then her throat was completely clear, and she began to drink water immediately, and soon after she ate food. Therefore, I did not need to incise that abscess for a long time, and an abscess formed in the place of that one, which I incised before it could spread further.
Then I had some vinegar and cauterized her throat with it; this was for the addition of treatment. And then I wandered around and did not know what God would do next regarding that situation.
Chapter xxxvii.
On incising an abscessed armpit.
When there is a discharge from the armpit, and it is abscessed and long, it is then called perpendicular; and if it is below thick and round, it is then called a bubo.
However, when it is cured by what we have mentioned in the division and does not conform to your treatment, and also sometimes an abscess remains inside but is subtle, then you should incise the one that is aggregated and round; and if it reaches a certain length and is bloody, of dark color or black, and does not respond at the center, then you should attempt the incision cautiously, for there is fear of malignancy in such cases.
And when you see it according to the method I have described regarding its whiteness and length, make the patient lie down with their arm raised using an instrument that fits the shape of the area, and then press it firmly. Then pull it downwards and incise it with one of two instruments mentioned in the double incision procedure.
And you should not incise it beyond what is appropriate within the natural limits without excess, for if you incise too much, you will harm the patient.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended in the double incision procedure, and care for it until it is healed.
However, if the patient is distressed by the incision, you should use cautery without fear, and the method of cauterization involves only sharp water. Place the head of the patient on your lap, then press it firmly with the instrument we have mentioned. Then take the sharp water that we described in the chapter on cauterization and apply it to the area, making sure not to make it too thick or thin, and fill the concavity of the instrument which has this shape:
Here is the extract of the cautery in which the medicine is placed as with the cautery of copper alloy, and place the instrument with the medicine on the wound, and the patient lies on his back so that saliva or mucus from the mouth does not fall onto it due to its proximity, lest it should cause harm to that part.
And then take the medicine and apply it to the uvula for a short period until you see it become blackened and numb from the bite of the medicine.
And if you wish, take a cork and wrap it around the end of the cautery, and dip the cork into the medicine, and insert the cautery with the cork into a tube so that the cork adheres to that tube, and do this as many times until you achieve what you desire from the cauterization of the wound, and then leave it until it dries and falls off after three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, do so, and then after the cauterization clean around the wound with a cork dipped in syrup, and modify it with what is around the wound from the medicine, and wash it with fresh water, and treat it externally with an embrocation, and care for it with remedies until it is healed.
And this kind of treatment is safer than incision and longer in duration out of caution.
In some cases, the uvula is cured with something that is less harsh than cauterization and incision, as it is treated with sublimation in this manner: take camphor, hyssop, oregano, rue, Arabian saffron, chamomile, and similar herbs, and mix all these ingredients or some of them in a pot, and immerse them in sour wine, and boil; the pot should be heated moderately, and in the middle of the pot place a tray upon which is placed an instrument made of wire like this: it should be made of silver or wire.
And let the extremity be inserted into the mouth of the patient until the vapor reaches the uvula above which the uvula is obscured.
And then proceed with it on those moist areas until they are dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the occurrence of abscesses, for it would be too harsh on the abscess, and you should not do this, but rather wait for the maturation of the abscess.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, now take a cane and attach to the end of it a piece of cloth dipped in vinegar, so that the mouth of the patient is protected, for the cloth dipped in vinegar prevents the steam from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
On the extraction of spines and of what adheres in the wound and of other causes.
Moist substances adhering to the wound or thorn or some other cause.
Then you should remove what is apparent from those substances, on which your eye rests, and then press the tongue with an instrument in front of sunlight, so that it becomes clear to you what is in the wound.
And what does not appear and is hidden in the wound, you should make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for the cause attached to the wound will be easily expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of bread or lettuce seed or a mouthful of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sea sponge and soak it well in vinegar, then let the patient swallow that, as when it reaches the place where spines are lodged, they will be easily expelled. Take one eel and boil it in vinegar, then remove its intestines and extract the contents; or take a poultice and apply the contents to it and spread it on.
If you do not forget the causes which we have mentioned, now use an instrument of lead according to this form:
And let it be as thick as a small finger, and at its end let there be a curve which you insert into its throat with ease.
And when you act, keep his head raised up, and be careful not to touch the bone of his throat, so that it does not injure him or the person who is holding him, or cause harm to the surgeon’s hand.
However, let the patient be well prepared, because of his knowledge of the location of the cause attached and held down, and pull his hand with the instrument up, all this according to the amount that is prepared within him so that it is not forgotten.
Chapter XXXIX.
On the extraction of a bloodsucker attached to the throat.
When treating a bloodsucker with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and not compresses, now consider the patient's throat in the sunlight, since you have already drawn his tongue forward with the instrument that we recommended.
Then if you see the bloodsucker, extract it gently with a small incision or with fine tweezers.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it then, now take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and immediately place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this as many times (until the bloodsucker is dead and falls off the throat) and keep the patient from water for the entire day.
Take a sponge full of clean water and wipe the mouth with it, then rinse the mouth with that water; and do not let any impurity enter from there, and pour out the water after an hour or so when the blood has settled.
But if it is not possible to do as we have recommended with this, then fumigate the malodor with cypress or with sweet basil using the instrument which we have recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, and he will be cured.
And there is another method of operation in fumigation for this, which is that you take an olive in which are burning coals and the olive is covered with a cover in the middle of which is a hole, and attach the end of the instrument to that hole; then proceed with the fumigation from above; and place the patient's mouth on the end of the tube, and close their mouth so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has reached the blood, for the blood will fall immediately.
And if it does not work, then repeat the fumigation as many times as necessary, and abstain the patient from drinking water, and give salty and dry foods, and let them not drink water; and it is also necessary that they follow this regimen strictly.
And this is the form of the instrument with which blood is drawn from the gum when it is seen; and it resembles forceps as you might see them, but with some curvature: which is placed in the gum, and the end of it resembles the beak of a heron, which is sharpened with a file, so that when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter XL.
In which is the summary of the speech on the preparation for abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are various types, of many ways, as will be recalled, each in this book.
And they differ in their puncture and incision by two methods, of which one is according to the nature of the abscess itself; and by what they derive from the humidity.
And the second method is according to the locations where they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from one occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess occurring in fleshy parts is different from one occurring at a joint; and each has its treatment by surgical operation.
And there are some abscesses that should not be punctured but only at the proper time of maturation of the pus or when they are fully developed.
There are other abscesses that should be evacuated when they are crosswise and not mature according to their development, such as abscesses near joints, because when an abscess is close to a joint, its nature extends until you can puncture what is around it, and lightly the ligaments or nerves of that joint may be corrupted, causing contraction of that limb; or if it is next to a principal member, delaying its puncture could lead to damage to the main member; or if it is near the buttocks, then puncture it before it becomes mature; but do not puncture it raw and without proper digestion, except for those close to the buttocks, so that you do not cause premature discharge of pus inside the buttocks, leading to an elongation and forming a fistula, or causing damage before it can heal. You know that you should pierce the abscess when it is ripe.
And you know that it is ripe when there is a reduction in pain and the cessation of fever, and shortly after, a decrease in swelling and pulse, and the sharpness at the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And you should know that the puncture should be made at a place where the pus is closest to the surface of the abscess and from where it will come out easily during healing.
And the puncture should always be made along the length of the skin or body if the abscesses are in parts such as the chest, elbows, tendons, cords of nerves, arteries; and finally, in all places equally where there is no reflection; for in those that do reflect, use a puncture according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy area, the best time to make the puncture is after the digestion of the second stage has been confirmed, as we have said; for if you pierce it before that, it will prolong the illness of the patient and cause much harm, and greatly harden the lips of the abscess and its depth.
But some abscesses should be punctured across the grain of the skin or body due to necessity, or according to what is required by the member.
And you should administer a small puncture, and in large abscesses, a larger puncture or multiple sections according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And other abscesses in which the incision should be hollowed and made when it is to be done as with bread, and it is done at a time when it can already stand, just like those that occur with excessive boils and buboes.
And of those that are cut open having three angles; and of those in which it is cut open as the figure of a myrtle leaf, just like the abscesses of the armpits.
And of those in which round incision and lunar incision are administered, and similar to these.
But in the one that does not have any such as its superficial lion abscesses, you should pierce it with a superficial puncture, or just plane it.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected pus, you should pierce it but do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that moment; having extracted one part from it, then scrape the abscess internally on another day, and then extract a larger portion, and continue doing this several times according to its progression until it heals.
And especially if the patient is a robust man, or a pregnant woman, or an infant, or an elderly person in frailty, for in such cases the vital spirit is quickly resolved through the evacuation of the pus, and the patient easily recovers, and you will have operated successfully.
Therefore, guard this chapter with great caution, and when you perform the incision in which you pierce these abscesses, make sure to clean the wounds and then consider whether the wound of the abscess is small or requires a flat incision; if it is small, administer some lint or antiseptic cotton. If the abscess is large and the incisions are many, then you should place dry lint in each tract until they heal.
And if in the abscess a part of the cord is cut or has been hollowed out along its circumference, then you should fill it with old cloth or thread made from linen, as when you have treated ulcers or swellings without moisture; and strengthen it on the third day; and subsequently proceed to treat it according to what is needed from ointments until it heals.
If blood flows during the time of your operation, then prepare water and vinegar in which you dip a cloth, and place that over the area where the blood is flowing for several times.
If the bleeding continues, then you should administer treatment and care, using powders which I have recommended in various places of this our book and in division.
If it is in the time of winter, and the site of the abscess is on many nerves, then you should apply powders mixed with vinegar and heated oil, and place those on the sites.
And if it is in summer, and the sites are fleshy, then you should apply powders soaked in water and oil or in vinegar and oil, and this should be wiped on for three days as we have said, and then you should open the abscess, cleanse it, and administer to its cure what it needs until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses generally, but according to the method of distinction I have already said about every abscess in what manner is the way of its cure separately by chapters.
Chapter XLIV.
On the section of abscesses that appear on the neck.
On the neck of the head, small abscesses appear, and they are of various types of nodes that contain membranes; and their types vary.
And they are soft, and the contents of these are moist, similar to jelly. Of those things which contain humidity like dust, and similar to them; and of those things which are lithic, hard.
And in the section of all, and in the extraction of those there is no fear, as you should know that the extraction of those does not oppose the curvature.
And the operation in the section of those is that you should press them with an instrument whose form approaches this, which is called an entrodes, so that you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then you should divide that with a section along its length by a superficial section according to this form:
And begin in the section from line .B. to line .G. Therefore, when the humidity is evacuated, you should then excise that skin which contains the humidity, and do not leave any cause for recurrence, as closely observed tumors return if any cause remains.
And then immerse a piece of cloth in Egyptian ointment if it is available, and if not, in water and salt; and apply it to the wound and leave it there for another day, as this will corrode everything that remains of that membrane.
And then apply the cloth to the ointment again, a second and third time if necessary, until you are confident that there is no remaining cause of that membrane; now treat the wound with an ointment until it is healed.
And if an abscess contains hard nodes, then you should incise that according to this form: with a section having the shape of a cross, and insert once between the two lips of the incision and scrape it thoroughly from all sides, and exert your skill in extracting the membrane that contains it.
If there is opposition from your surgeon, now do what I have recounted first.
And similarly treat the mature abscess by incision and cure as we have told you; and the incision of a mature abscess is easier, since it contains little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are cases of abscesses in the head in certain people who are not very moist; the cause is that I once incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found it as hard as a stone, rough, white, which could not be penetrated, and if anyone were to attempt to treat it that way, they would suffer harm.
However, in other abscesses that occur in the head, this is not the case of this chapter; also, the abscesses that appear on the heads of children and behind the ears should be treated with superficial incisions.
And pause the incision of those in the lower part so that the contents of the abscess can drain into the lower parts.
And then treat those as is appropriate for that type of cure.
Chapter XLII.
On the incision of scrofulas which occur in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck and armpits; and sometimes they appear in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck often develop between the muscles, and every scrofula of these is within its own proper layer, just as with knots and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
There are various types of these scrofulas, and they are spots. Of those, only some are lithic, and of those, only the ones containing moisture are subject to treatment, and among them are malignant ones which do not respond to cure.
Therefore, when you encounter one that is in good condition during the examination, whose color matches that of the gland and which is not adhered to any part, nor attached to the neck nerves or veins, and is not swollen, cut it with dry scissors superficially above the upper line of .G. between the line of .B., and scrape it completely from both sides of the gland as we have said about abscesses of the head, and remove it little by little.
And it is more careful that the vein or nerve not be cut.
And let the scraping not be sharp, so as not to harm you during cutting or disturb the patient, and do not cut what is not necessary; for if you were to cut a vein or artery and stop your operation, then apply green vitriol powder or other powders that promote blood flow and staunch the wound. Let it rest inside where the abscess subsides, and when the inflammation is mollified and begins to become purulent, proceed accordingly. When the blood has been constricted.
Therefore, return to your operation once you have completed it.
And then inquire at the time of the operation if there is any cause for other small scrofulas, so cut those and clean them.
And if it is in the root of the scrofula a large vein, cut at the hour when you do not injure that scrofula from its root. And cut so that the seam is very strong and let it be such that it falls by itself without harm.
And then fill the wound with old cloth which you have already soaked in Egyptian oil, and then treat that.
And if you cut out that scrofula entirely, then sew together the two lips of the wound and close it up so quickly that there is no time for any superfluity.
But if you see that there is excessive superfluity due to the size of the scrofula, then cut into its root and extract it, apply sutures, and do as has been said.
And for the scrofulas that contain humidity, pierce it with a superficial incision where the place of maturation appears to you, and proceed with an incision that reaches the deepest part of the mass, as we have said.
And then proceed with the incision, apply ointment and treatments suitable for those cases, so that it accelerates what remains of the corruption. And when you see the wound already cleaned, then treat it with ointment of flesh-generating properties until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
On the incision of the pustule of an accidental abscess in the groin.
The ancients called this section in the epitome, and no one in our land has seen that operation performed.
And this is the inscription of the speech of those people.
In men who have a squint, it is advisable to avoid the extraction of the epilhot, as by doing this no benefit will be gained since all the veins and the major ones in the affected area are not reached.
In those who have boils in the mouth or throat or near the ears, when the illness is not in the bone, it is better to use the section of epilhot to avoid the loss which is caused by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the epilhot below three circles of its circles, or four if it is a small section across two circles in quantity. The section should be made in soft tissue, not in cartilage, and this place is suitable for cutting because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels there are few.

And let the wound be left open for some time.

On the Section of Kinds of Knots.

This may not be a heat nor fever nor pain, and it continues like a lion's leap which is to its prey.
And it is according to the color of the body, and the beginning of it is like a pustule and it is made thus as it vacates, whether larger or smaller, and its kinds are two, either dry or greasy and containing moisture, and the colors of the moisture are motley, as I say in the division.

If anything remains inside for any reason, whether little or much, the wound will return according to the vein.

Chapter XLVII.
On the treatment of men's breasts that are similar to women's.

And for that which you are to treat in this manner, cut it into a crescent-shaped section according to this form, from the line of B to the line of G, and then shave off all the roughness, and then fill the wound with flesh-growing medicine, and then sew together the two edges of the wound, and care for it until it is healed.

Chapter XLIX.
On the incision of abscesses that occur due to arteries or veins.
When an artery is ruptured and the part above it becomes inflamed, an abscess may occur. The signs by which one can determine whether inflammation and abscess are due to an artery or a vein: for when the abscess is due to an artery, it tends to aggregate in the upper part of the body; and if you press on the abscess with your fingers, you will hear a hissing sound in it.
And when it is due to a vein, it appears near the surface of the body.
The incision of this abscess is risky, especially in areas such as the armpits, angles, neck, and other parts of the body; it is dangerous, so its treatment should be avoided, and particularly so if it occurs in the extremities or on the head.
But for an abscess caused by inflammation of the artery opening, incise it in its proper section lengthwise; then carefully open the section with hooks near the artery, and separate the tunics until it is exposed.
Upon that needle I place something and make it penetrate into the other side, and tie the artery with double thread in two places, which cause I explain in the extraction of two arteries, which are in the temples.
And then place the location between two ligatures with a spatum inside so that the blood stops there until it is all dissolved and the abscess is resolved.
And then administer treatment in which pus is generated so that the ligatures fall off; and afterward, treat it with appropriate remedies so that it heals.
And if it is an abscess due to the section of a vein, then you should hold with your hand what is within reach concerning the abscess and its condition, and then insert the needle below the place where you have held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and tie a double thread in such a way that you extract it from the other side, and afterward ligate the abscess with a good ligature as I have told you regarding the grapevine ligature on both sides of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be extracted, then insert the thread with the needle below all of the abscess beyond the insertion of the first needle, and tie the threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in its proper place so that when what is inside it comes out, you then incise the excess of the vessel, and leave what is connected to it.
And then apply powder on which you have soaked in vinegar and oil, and afterward administer the treatment with bandages and remedies so that it heals.
Chapter on abscesses which occur due to nerve strain.
Just as abscesses occur in arteries and veins, so too do abscesses occur in nerves when they are struck or due to excessive labor, and similarly.
And it happens that this occurs in the areas of the joints or the heel, or in any place where the joints are frequently troubled.
It is a severe abscess and resembles the color of the surrounding tissue. It is one of its worst conditions when it is hard and accompanied by vehemence, and at that time the patient appears in a state similar to stupor.
And it is not an abscess that aggregates at the front of the body, and because of this it is less severe, and it affects all parts equally without going to the parts in front or behind.
Therefore, when it is in the joints, it should not be treated with iron, for it would easily lead to contraction.
But when it is in the head or on the forehead, treatment should be done with spatum. But if the abscess is small, then at this time treat it with forceps and incise it carefully.
But if it is large, then suspend it with ligatures, and excise it, and afterward close the wound; and adjust the wound with stitches, and care for it until it heals.
Chapter II.
On the section of abscesses which occur due to pain in the belly.
Thus, for some persons, swellings occur in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies which are called fungi because they resemble mushrooms; the root of these is delicate and their head is thick. And often the lips of these are smooth, and it is of those that are small, and it is of those whose magnificence is great.
And I myself have seen one, and in his belly there were two parts which resembled mushrooms more closely, and there was no difference between those white ones with their delicate roots.
And the lips of those were turned outward and split open, and moisture flowed from them all the time.
Therefore, I incised them and found within one weighing about eighteen uncias and the other weighing about six uncias.
And the procedure in incising them is that you consider whether the malady is humid, and if the color of the tumor is white, humid, with delicate roots; then you incise it with a broad scalpel, and immediately in your presence cauterize it over fire, for the moisture and swelling must be expelled by drying up the blood; and if there is much bleeding, cauterize that.
And if you find the malady rare and fearful, guard against incising with an iron instrument; rather, take fresh lead, apply it to that tumor in the manner we have described; and leave it for two days.
And immediately adjust the tightness of the lead band; therefore, do not stop applying the lead continuously until it is detached and falls off by itself.
If there is a thick pus in the wound, at that moment when making the incision into it there is some cause for fear and dread, especially if it is in the abdomen.
It is possible that you may incise half of it, and then cauterize that so that it does not return.
And then treat it internally until the wound is healed.
And be careful not to presume to incise pus that is of a dark color, with little sensation, and hard in consistency, for that is usually cancerous.
And this will come as a reminder regarding these matters.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of an umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia is caused by various reasons; it may be due to fissures in the abdominal wall above the belly, and through these openings, either tissue or intestines protrude as in other ruptures; or it can be caused by blood that flows from a vein or artery depending on what is present there; or by trapped air.
However, if it is due to a fissure in the abdominal wall and the protrusion of tissue, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the skin; it is soft without pain, and may appear in various positions. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, then its appearance differs greatly from what we have described, and when you press on it with your fingers, it retreats and then returns; it is soft to the touch, and becomes more prominent after bathing and vigorous work.
Meats that are moist, when pressed with your hand, do not remain hidden, nor are they constricted, nor are they hard.
And if it is due to inflammation, then these signs appear: the abscess turns to a declining blackness.
Meat that is badly grown results in a severe, large, and hard abscess, which penetrates according to the amount.
If it is due to gas, then the touch of it is light.
And the operation in this case is that you consider whether the prominence of the navel is due to inflammation of an artery, or a vein, or gas; then it is advisable for you to be cautious about treating it, since there is fear and dread as I have taught you in the chapter where I mentioned abscesses from arteries and veins.
And if the prominence of the navel is due to the bowel or membrane, then it is advisable for you to command the patient to lie on their back and extend themselves; then examine around the entire navel with your hand, and then instruct them to stand between your hands on their back; then encircle the area with a piece of linen in the circumference of the navel where you have felt with your hand; and then extend half of the abscess above about an inch; and then tie it in the circumference tightly with strong thread or string according to the proper method.
Then pierce the center of the abscess extended over the bandage and insert your finger into that area to feel for the bowel; and if you find it caught or held by the bandage, then carefully extract it and move the bowel away from inside the abdomen.
And if you have found the cloth, now take one ounce of it; and incise the superfluities from it.
And if it is contrary to your art or vein, now surround that spot, and return to your operation; and take two needles and place in those two strong points and interpose two needles in the circuit which you have made around the abscess in a crisscross manner, which you should make penetrate; and then press the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area so that the extended flesh can drain and fall off on its own; or incise it when it is softened and can be drained, and then treat it with whatever medicine is needed until it is healed.
And if the eminence of the navel is of flesh or due to moisture, now it is necessary that you conquer the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or moisture which you find there, and then treat it with what is needed to heal and consolidate the area.
Chapter LIII.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
We have already discussed in the division, the types of wounds and how to treat them with medicines, and that it is important for the treatment to avoid infection so that the wound does not become ulcerated; and we have also explained the causes and precautions in the treatment of wounds.
And the ancients said that in some cases wounds can be treated in places where it is possible to remove them completely, just as with wounds in the breasts or armpits and similar parts of the body assigned to extract everything, especially when they are starting small.
When you are old and gray, do not seek to undertake it, for I cannot heal from that illness nor have I seen another, nor has anyone else reached that.
And the operation is performed as we have said, when you proceed and leave the patient to purge from black bile several times, and then bleed him if retention is found to be evident.
And then prepare the patient with the preparation in which the operation will take place, and then hold up hooks that are needed for it; and then wrap all around with a good cloth to prevent any cause of infection inside so that no reason for it remains. And let the blood flow freely, and do not cut it hastily, and press the areas, and wipe away the thick blood with your hand or with an instrument that is suitable among the available tools.
But if it is contrary for thick blood to coagulate in a vein or artery, then cauterize that area until the flow of blood stops, and afterward treat it with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 46.
On the treatment of hydrops (edema or dropsy).
We have already recommended, in the division, the types of hydrops related to water, and the signs of each type, as well as the treatment of it with medicines.

And when you have already treated this kind of tympanitic hydrops with medicines and do not confine your treatment, now consider whether the patient has reached debility, or if it is another illness accompanied by hydrops, just as if it were in the chest or abdomen similar to those cases. Then guard yourself that you do not treat them with iron, for there is fear concerning this.
If you see the patient with abundant vigor, and in them there is no other illness but hydrops alone, and they are neither a child nor elderly, then the method of the operation on them is that you lift the patient between your arms, and the assistant presses the abdomen of the patient with their hand, expelling the water from below the navel, and then take a spatum spinos (a sharp instrument) in this manner:
It should be of two sharp parts long, with a sharp tip like a spatum; however, it should have some slight tapering little by little, so that in the operation you do not pass into the bowel, and thus injure it.
And then consider whether the origin of the hydrops is from the bowels, for then it is necessary that in your section you keep away from the navel by a distance of three fingers below, on the right side over the navel.
But if the origin of the hydrops is from disease of the kidney, then your section should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if the origin of it is from the spleen, then your section should be on the right side.
And it is not necessary that your section be on the side on which the patient lies, so that the fluids do not rush to that place and cause harm.

About balanitis, which occurs in the foreskin at the head of the penis, and about corruption, blackness, and adherence of the foreskin to the head of the penis.

Chapter LXVIII.
On the softening of the cord of the testicles.
In many cases, the cord of the testicles is softened in some men because the flesh that lies within it is softened, and this requires careful attention.
Then it is necessary for the one who is asked to treat this condition to have the patient lie according to his side, and incise the entire cord which is softened over the natural cord; and then adjust two edges with suturing.
And if you wish, cut away the excess of the softened cord at three or four places and secure it with a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and afterwards treat it as you would other wounds until it is healed and the pus subsides.
Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Know that castration according to our law is excommunicated, but for the reason it is read to me wherefrom I remember it in this book; and the remembrance of it is not necessary except for two ways, of which one is so that when asked about it by someone who suffers from it, the physician knows how to cure it; and the other way is that we sometimes have need of castration in other animals for our use through them, such as horses, oxen, and pigs, and similar to such animals.
And thus I say that castration is performed in two ways: either by cutting off, or by dividing and sectioning.
But in the one that is performed by cutting off, the method of the operation is that we should scald the animal in hot water until the testicles become soft and are loosened so that they can be removed. And then take those [testicles] with your hand until they are separated and do not appear below the touch.
But in castration performed by division and sectioning, hold the animal and press the scrotum of the animal with your left hand.
And then lift the suspensory ligaments and make an incision on each side such that two processes are exposed.
And then cut those parts after you have peeled each one. And do not leave on those parts any cause for infection, but make the castration subtle so that it is above the veins.
And this method of castration performed by cutting off is better, because cutting off leaves some cause from the testicles for which the animal desires to copulate.
Regarding the treatment of internal wounds, let them be healed.
Chapter LXX.
On the treatment of hermaphrodism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two kinds: the first appears where there is a space between the testicles, resembling a female form with characteristics such as hair.
And in some cases, that same place through which urine passes is located between the testicles.
In women, there is one kind: the part of the female organ above the pubis appears as two testicles of a man, slightly recessed as if it were between the thighs like the scrotum and testicles of a man.
The treatment is of three kinds: two for men and one for women. It is that you should cut away the tight tissues so as to remove the cause of that condition, and then treat it as you would other wounds.
And in the second kind of men, where urine passes through a space between the testicles, there is no treatment or surgery for it.
Chapter XXI.
On the treatment of protrusions and raised flesh due to causes related to women.
A protrusion that is slightly loose due to natural causes should be treated and managed carefully; in some cases, it becomes enlarged in women to the extent that it extends similarly to men and interferes during intercourse.
Then you should have on hand the excess of linting with your hand or with a lancet.
And do not stop at the incision, especially at the root of the hair, so that there is no flow of blood.
And immediately take care of that during the treatment of the wounds until it is healed.
Meat born is meat that grows in the hollow of the mother until it fills that space, and easily spreads to other parts like a tail (and for this reason some first called it "tail disease"); therefore, we should incise it as we would with linting, and take care of it until it is healed.
Chapter LXXII.
On the Treatment of Pterygium.
Pterygium is when the woman's hymen is not perforated or the opening is small. And it can be either natural or accidental.
It is accidental due to a disease that preceded it, and that condition is caused either by loose flesh or by thick phlegm.
And it occurs either at the root of the uterus, or on its sides, or in the upper part of it, or in the lower part, and it prevents intercourse and conception and childbirth, and also easily disrupts menstruation.
And the diagnosis of that condition is made by examination with a speculum, if the obstruction is apparent; but if it is not apparent, you should seek it with your fingers or with a probe.
If there is an obstruction and it is small, and it is near the two lips, then you should cut it and disrupt that.
And it is that you place on the two lips pellets, and close by you place the fingers called indicators of both the nearest ones.
And the woman should lie on her back, with her legs spread apart, and stretch the two lips firmly so that the small obstruction is broken and the blockage is opened.
And then take some linen and soak it in oil and place that on the site, and instruct the patient to leave it as is so that the cut area does not heal too quickly.
But if the obstruction is large and thick, then you should cut that with a scalpel or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to flesh, then suspend that for a moment, and cut it open.
And you should have the medicines that stop blood flow without causing harm, such as hellebore, dragon's blood, and feathers, mixed with fresh egg white.
And then administer a lead tube that is wide so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep that for several days and administer dry lint.
And then proceed with the other treatment for healing until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the greater part of other tissues, and then you should cut according to this method. If this is due to an abscess in the vein, do not make any opposition to it by applying iron in any way.
Chapter LXXIII.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids and tumors that occur in the anus of women.
Hemorrhoids are an inflammation of the openings of small veins, through which blood flows continuously; and when these hemorrhoids become old, they form thrombi.
And now I have mentioned in the division the types of those conditions and the signs of them, and to say which of them receive treatment and which do not receive treatment.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and thrombi when they are in the vicinity of the vein and do not appear to the senses, at this time no treatment should be performed with iron on them.
However, those that are in the opening of the vein, on which one can feel a sensation, are those that can be treated.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a suitable position and then grasp the hemorrhoid with forceps or with a rough cloth, and incise them with razors.
And then powder should be applied to stop the flow of blood from them using one of the powders for incisions without causing irritation, such as acacia and similar substances.
And then bind the area with a dressing for one hour, and afterward immerse it in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then have the patient sit in a decoction of sumac and pomegranate peels and gallnuts and similar substances.
Take barley flour and mix it with honey and vinegar to make a plaster, which you should apply to the back.
And if the bleeding has stopped, that is good; otherwise, apply it to both sides of the navel and on the nipples without scarifying.
And take wool and soak it in the juice of coriander or in the juice of anise or in the juice of hyssop with vinegar, then apply it to the affected area; afterwards, treat the area with internal remedies until it is healed.
And treat it internally until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIV.
On the piercing of the uterus in accidental cases involving the mother.
Various types of abscesses occur in the mother, such as carbuncles, stone-like abscesses, ulcerous abscesses, hemorrhoidal abscesses, rectal abscesses, fistulas, hemorrhoids, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses and their types and the signs of them, as well as their treatments in detail.
And what I say in this treatise about abscesses applies to those that occur in the mother when they are among the abscesses that can be treated successfully, and if you want to know how the procedure is performed with iron.
Then you must consider whether the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute upon percussion and phlegm and fever, and if there is redness and sensitivity in that area, then do not proceed with piercing it, but treat it with what helps internal maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms subsides.
During the hours when you wish to perform circumcision on a woman, place her on a bed that has two supports; and recline her on her back and arrange her feet at the part below the belly, and spread out her thighs, and position her arms above her head, and bind some of them with bandages appropriately.
Let the midwife follow on the right side and administer the instrument with which the opening of the birth canal is stretched, the form of which will be described in the cases that will be mentioned.

If the instrument is larger than the opening of the birth canal, then place pillows around the tissue of the birth canal's opening so that it prevents the insertion of any instrument into the birth canal.
And it is necessary to place the screw that runs along the side of the instrument, and let the practitioner hold the instrument while turning the screw so that the opening of the birth canal is stretched.
And when the wound appears and is touched by the hand and feels soft and moist, then it is necessary to dry that area with a dry cloth.

Take a piece of clean cloth and place it on the wound after dipping it in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And it should be treated twice a day with what is suitable for this until it is healed.
And sometimes the wound and the inflammation are washed with this so that it is cleansed with water and honey, and then with water in which lily root or iris root has been boiled, and then returned to the dressing.
And if the inflammation is deep in the wounded area, it is now necessary to avoid treating it with iron, instead using what has been described in the section on this type of care.
Chapter LXXV.

Chapter LXXVI.
On the Extraction of a Dead Fetus.
When you are treating a dead fetus as we have said in the division of medication and it has not yet been expelled, and you must use surgery to do so, then you should consider whether the woman is of sound constitution and not affected by any of those conditions for which she is feared to suffer loss.
At this point, the woman should be placed on her back on the bed with her buttocks raised and her head lowered.
And the minister should hold her by both legs or tie her to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you should moisten the mouth of the mother with oil, honey, and mucilage made from linseed, fenugreek, and flax seeds.
And then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then she should gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where to hook into the fetus.
And the appropriate places for this are as follows: then you should consider whether the fetus is positioned to descend head first, and if so, you should place the hooks in the hands of the fetus, or in the neck, or in the mouth, or on the palate, or under the chin, or at the clavicle, or in the proper places along the ribs, or under the armpits.
But if the descent is foot first, then in the buttocks, or in the middle of the back.
Take the needle in your right hand and place its curvature at that point which is between the fingers of your left hand, then insert it with ease into one of those places we have mentioned until it reaches the diseased area.
And then place and insert another needle beside it, or a third if necessary, so that there is equal attraction, and not extension according to its straightness alone.
And let it be avoided in all parts, so that the extraction of it is eased just as it is done with teeth during their extraction.
And it is necessary in the place where the extension should be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then the midwife should anoint some of those places with oil and insert them carefully and roll there what is retained.
Then if a part of the fetus is dislodged, the needles should be moved to a place that is more suitable; and do this until all of the fetus is dislodged.
But if the hand is dislocated in front of the other, and it is not possible to reduce it due to its contraction, then wrap a cloth around it; and afterwards extract it until it is completely dislodged, and either incise it at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And do the same for the other hand and both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or contraction occurs during its extraction, or it is in the head of the tight water bag, then insert between the fingers and cut open the head to extract the water, or use an instrument called “alundat,” the form of which will be among the forms of other instruments in the chapter that follows this one.
And it seems that if the bone is large by nature: then at this time it must be sawed through with a chain-saw or taken out as we have said; and next remove from the bones what remains with the forceps.
And if the bone is bent and is compressed at the fork, then it should be split so that the moisture which is in the bones may be effused.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it protrude abnormally, then you should saw the bone immediately as soon as it is possible for you.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscessed or in it there is dropsy, then you should puncture that area through which flows the moisture that is within.
But if the bone is on the ribs, then the attraction of it is distant and the direction of it to the mouth of the artery is distant.
But if it is compressed against the abdomen and the bones, then you should take out that blade which you have wrapped around your hand, and cut open the abdomen and the bones through which flows what is within them.
But if the other members remain still and the bone returns, then place your left hand in between; but if the mouth of the artery is open, then insert your hand into the wound, and search for the bone, and pull it with the said instrument into the mouth of the artery, and then insert an inch or two inches, and pull it with those.
And if the mouth of the artery is compressed by an abscess that has occurred, then it should not be touched directly; instead, you should administer moist and fatty applications. Apply plasters; and have the patient use waters that soften and moisten.
And if the condition is on the side, then if possible, puncture it; and administer what we have said about the living patient.
And if that is not possible, then cut it into pieces, and extract it.
And make sure not to leave any cause inside for the second time.
And when you have completed your entire operation, then administer what we have said about the treatment for boils that are similar to those that occurred previously.
And if there is bleeding, then bathe in astringent waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb there was a dead fetus, and above that focus another pregnancy occurred, and the other fetus was also dead. It happened to one who had for a long time a boil in their navel area; and it became swollen inside with a pus-filled focus, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to treat them, and treated them for a long time, but the plague did not heal.

Of the extraction of the second [incisor].

On the treatment of the unopened anus of a child.
Some children are born with their anuses not pierced, which are small and closed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to carefully pierce that mentioned opening with her finger; and if not, she should use a sharp probe to pierce it, being careful not to touch the membrane.
And then apply lint soaked in wine and oil to it; and subsequently treat the area until it is healed.

On the treatment of fistulas that occur below.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part, close to the body or near the buttocks outside of it or in the area between them, tend to be hard and thick.
And if it is one fistula or multiple.
And as that hardness subsides and there is a discharge of watery white liquid or subtle pus, sometimes these fistulas penetrate further.
And these penetrating ones are known by what is felt during discharge and by windiness; and sometimes the discomfort of discharge indicates that they are indeed such.
Of those fistulas, some extend into the space penetrating into the bladder, and into the urethra of the penis; and some of them penetrate into the joint of the thigh and into the root of the bone.
And in those that are known as penetrating fistulas into the rectum and non-penetrating ones, it is when you place your finger in the rectum, and insert a fine probe of hair or iron into the fistula where there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in that area, then insert a fine probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair with your finger.
And if you do not feel anything of this nor find any cause for evacuation, such as worms or gas, as we have said, then you know that it is non-penetrating, and at this point the treatment should be applied.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the urethra of the penis, then the significance of this is that urine will come from it, and it prohibits such that the site must be treated to consolidate.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the area, then the sign of this is that the probe passes through here if there is no tortuosity within the bone, and causes pain in the circumference of the affected area; and that you will have pus continuously from it; and that medication does not help in this case, nor can it be consolidated by treatment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the treatment of these is hard work and futility for physicians, as such is their fate.
In that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing with iron is hoped for as I will tell you, and this which I have already promised.
And it is that the patient should lie between your thighs with their buttocks higher and elevate the limbs within them, the objects being inclined towards the belly of the patient, and then introduce a probe made of lead or iron if it is in the fistula tortuosity, until you know the place where the probe passes.
And if that is the case where the patient surrounds the anus, then you should introduce your finger into the anus.
And if you feel with your finger that the probe has already been discovered penetrating, or what you feel between your finger and that tube or flesh, then you know certainly that it is penetrating; and then do not work on that one, for there is no healing in that one, as we have said.
But some might say that it heals in some men slowly.
And for the treatment in which hope is placed is that you use a subtle cautery according to the size of the fistula, as has been described, and introduce that heated instrument into the fistula until it goes around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those inflamed tissues have become constricted like a feather quill.
And then treat that with poultices wrapped in cloth until all those constricted tissues heal, and then treat that with medicines that generate and consolidate the flesh, and if it heals, good; but if not, then it will not heal at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters and does not penetrate further, tell which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a membrane of flesh or ciphac [type of cloth], and sometimes the fistula is on what dries the surface of the intestine. And then treat that with bandages wrapped in butter or honey until the area gets better, and afterwards cure the wound with a hard flesh graft that generates and strengthens.
And medicines that have the property of being styptic and drying; when these are restored, it is healed and you need not fear recurrence.
