On the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that every wound and abscess when it matures and becomes old, and forms an ulcer, and is not consolidated and continues to discharge for a long time without being excised, whether in any limb, is universally called a fistula, and we refer to it as that abscess.
And a fistula truly is a felted node, hard, white, at which there is no pain and to which there is adherence as there is adherence of bird's nest; and for this reason it is called that rough swelling, that is, sore.
And it is in some people a moist swelling, which discharges continuously; and the moisture is excised within a few hours. And at times this moisture is pus, and it is little, and it is thick, and it is thin.
And I have shown all those causes, the signs of them, and their cure with medicines in the division.
And it is appropriate that I inform you about the treatment of fistulas, which is performed through surgery with my methods.
And you should know that fistulas can occur in all the members of the body; among them are those that extend to large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spine, or the intestines, or the bladder, or any of the ribs, or some of the vertebrae of the back, or any of the complex joints such as those of the hand and foot; and there are also those that affect the vicinity of major limbs and similar areas.
And therefore, among these fistulas that follow this manner, they are among the illnesses difficult to heal, and do not easily receive a cure, and dealing with them requires effort and ignorance.
And if any of the fistulas has a depth which does not reach to the places mentioned above and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or of steel, and if the fistula goes according to straightness, explore it with that.
And if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of thin lead, for the lightness of its body causes it to be easily guided and curved around the tortuosity.
And if the fistula is having movements of horror, it is not possible for you to signify upon it with a probe.
Then anoint the openings through its openings, for the moisture with which you anoint it will go to the other openings and spread throughout them.
And then inquire very carefully according to the method by which you can know if here there is bone or nerve, and whether the depth of the fistula is distant or near, or if it is a fistula with all its openings and lies over everything within your power, within your inquiry, within which you inquire about the malady, and the cause of the pain lies under your compression on the place, and similar signs and accidental causes to the abscess.
And when you have stood upon all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the cure according to confidence: and that is that you consider now whether it is a fistula apparent near or in a salt place, distant from joint, or nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I have told you.
Now you dry the fistula according to what was said before about its narration, and remove what is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and what has grown in the opening of the flesh or tissue and similar parts, and treat it until it is healed.
If the fistula is of great depth and follows a straight course, it becomes necessary to incise it as far inward as possible, and then remove all corrupted flesh; afterwards apply poultices wrapped in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula which you have opened with iron.
And do this several times until the sharp medicine has dissolved everything that remains in the cavity of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with applications that produce healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it does not heal with this treatment, then you should cauterize it as we have recommended.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you need to determine this, then you should investigate it and dry it out as we have recommended, unless there is any prohibition against treating a major vessel, nerve, or primary limb as we have stated.

On the treatment of hemorrhoids and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members and the space of the abscess extends so that pus collects inside, and then it is broken open and perforated, and the pus is discharged from it, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin above it remains thin like cloth, and has not reached corruption, nor does it affect the bone or nerve or tendon, it is called a hemorrhoid, and it is not named a fistula.
However, if the space extends so that corruption affects some member, it is then called a fistula and not a hemorrhoid.
And in the treatment of this, incise that wound completely as it is revolved, and especially when it is subtle and made like cloth, and make sure that it does not adhere to the place by its corruption.
But if you hope that the wound will adhere and that such corruption has not yet succeeded by its later consequence, and there is a swelling in the wound, then treat it with an enema.
And it is that you consider the smell, and if it is great, and the arm that you have is of fetid odor, then treat it with an Egyptian medicine consisting equally of oil, vinegar, and raisin, which are mixed in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine becomes thick like honey.
And then take what is necessary and dissolve the medicine in water and honey; and administer it as an enema to the wound, and plug the opening of the wound, and leave the medicine inside for the duration of two hours.
And then take that medicine with care; and do this for several days until the wound is cleansed and the odor is removed.
And sometimes an enema is given with ash water when this medicine is not available to you; and it is that you take the ashes of wheat, put them over water; and then strain it and administer it as an enema until you are convinced that the wound is now clean.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then administer an enema with honey and water mixed with finely strained raisins; and give an enema of vinegar and honey mixed, for honey has the property to cleanse and wash, and vinegar helps the wound to adhere, especially when the vinegar contains a surplus of astringency and dryness.
And when you find that some areas are swollen and you confirm that the sinus has already burst, then irrigate that area with water that promotes the growth of flesh.
And it is that you should take a decoction of white figs and dissolve it with rose oil and vine stiptic; or what you irrigate that area with certain medicines and other decoctions which have the properties that I have already written about in the chapter on decoctions.
And if the orifice of the sinus is narrow and does not accept the cannula for irrigation, then you should gradually enlarge it with a small tool, and pause in there a cloth wrapped in Egyptian decoction or basilicon decoction until it is enlarged.
And similarly, if the orifice is too wide, then you should sew together the edges of that area with stitches and leave it in a quantity sufficient for the irrigation to take place.
And if the orifice from which the pus flows is elevated to the upper parts, then dry that area in the lower place where the pus can flow down, because when the pus is restrained in the depth of the sinus, flesh will grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus within the lower bone area as you wish, then prepare the member so that the pus flows freely according to your ability and ensure that there is no obstruction hindering the flow of the pus.
Place above the opening of the sinus, which you should probe at all times so that what is inside the arm may drain. And so that you may know whether what flows from the arm is thin or thick, or is undigested or digested.
And with this, you should consider the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and whether it is in an abscess.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter which is discharged over the opening of the sinus adheres or tends to adhere, then on the first day you should loosen it or do so every third day, and when you loosen the crust, then change the dressing and renew the application.
But if the sinus is in an area where there is subtle pus, then do not despair of its adhesion, for healing is appropriate, because some people experience a delay in the generation of flesh in their wounds, and others experience the opposite.
And if the sinus remains loose for several days and the arm is undigested, then you know that the sinus does not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and becomes prolonged, then adjust to the drying effect of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they should be as dry as a definitive dressing when applied, because there is an excess of moisture to be removed.
And the most important thing in curing is to take a definitive dressing and dissolve it in rose oil, and place it over the wound as an antiseptic in its substance, and use it appropriately.
In that action, when there is no bandage, find a piece of linen, take honey and mix it so that it thickens; or take myrrh and aloes and grind them up, sprinkle the powder over the honey which is on the fire, then stir until mixed well, spread this on cloth and stretch it over the wound; and put the linen with the thickened honey which you have cooked over the place of the injury, and sprinkle it on a cloth and apply that.
And sometimes administer with the medicine a long piece of linen and roots of lily (and it is celestial lily) and flour of oats and centaury, and use those simple ingredients or adjust them, grind them up and sieve them, then sprinkle the powder over the honey which has been recommended; and use that.
And if the wound is prolonged and has not been healed by this treatment, then you know that corruption is in its root, and it affects the bones or other hard members or the ligaments; now treat it with fistula treatment, as mentioned above.
On the treatment of paronychia and cracked nails, and the cutting of an added finger joint, and sectioning.
Paronychia is an abscess that arises from the nail of a toe or finger of the hand, and it can also occur in other fingers. When it becomes prolonged, its treatment is more difficult; it may be an abscess caused by cautery, or it may be corrupted. It generates pus inside which corrodes the root of the nail, often corrupting the entire area, sometimes spreading to the bone causing a foul odor, and making the extremity of the finger or toe rotten, with its color turning blackish.
Therefore, when you have that which we have said in the division and do not confirm your treatment, then it is necessary that you incise with iron all the superfluous part that is rotten from the nail and immediately cauterize the wound nearby, for cauterizing is helpful in such cases.
If the bone is exposed and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail have already compressed the flesh inward, and they begin to pierce and cause pain in that area, then you should place a thin probe from below the angle of the nail that pierces the flesh, and urge it up and incise that flesh with ease, and apply upon what remains of the flesh corrosive burning medicines until it is completely removed; and afterwards treat that with applications until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already spread to the bone, then you should incise the bone and extract it, for it is never healed when the bone is corrupted.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption spreading to the finger, then you should incise it at one of the joints according to where its removal is necessary, and afterwards treat it until it is healed.
But if there is an injury or irritation to the nail and there is severe pain in that area, then at the beginning you should bleed the patient, and afterwards dry the nail with a sharp spatula in a transverse cut from above down, and be careful that the cut does not reach into the flesh below the nail, for you would cause intense pain to the patient, and it would become a cause for the excessive growth of new flesh over the wound; and then treat that place with something that relieves pain.
This may be adjusted, which is natural in most people, being soft and fleshy everywhere, or soft and hollow in the bones, or soft and weak in the nails.
And some of them are born in the root of the joint, or the birth of it is in some nodes of the said joints. But that which is born in the nodes of the finger does no harm, whereas that which is soft and born in the joints may cause damage.
And of those that are born fleshy, the section is shallow, and it is such that you should incise them to the base with a blunt knife.
But for that which is born in the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; therefore, guard against its excision.
But in that which is born between the nodes of the finger, you should incise at the beginning the flesh around it with a round incision to the bone, and then close it with one of the appropriate sutures, and afterward care for what lies within until it heals.
But for consolidation that occurs between the bones due to their rubbing against each other or because of the birth condition of the person who is born this way, or due to inflammation or burning by fire, then you should section that consolidation until the bones return to their natural form.
And then place between their membranes in oiled linen so that they do not consolidate too quickly, and separate them, or place between them a thin sheet of lead until they are consolidated as needed.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in any of them on the palm, then you should section that consolidation according to how it is prepared and suits the shape of the member.
Of incisions for veins, such are varicose.
Veins are varicose, and veins wrapped thick with melancholic excesses that flow to the extremities of the body, and the greatest occurrence of them in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers and workers and carriers.
Then it is necessary that you administer purgation of the body from black bile several times with strong expulsion, and subsequently bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
The treatment for them is by two methods: one is that they be incised and the black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be elevated and completely extracted.
The sectioning is done in this way: first, immerse the leg in hot water for a moment so that the thick blood becomes resolved, which is sediment in the leg, and then stretch the patient's limb to the most prominent part of the swelling below the knee with a bandage, and then tie off the vein at one place, or two, or three with wide ligatures, and then draw out the black blood with your hand from below the knee upward into the thigh until the quantity is released that you wish and that suffices, and which the patient's strength can tolerate.
And then bandage it, and instruct him to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full, and inform the patient about this.
But the elevation of them is done in this way: first shave the leg of the patient if there are many hairs on it, and then place him in a bath or immerse the leg in hot water until it reddens and the veins become prominent; or have him exercise vigorously with exercise if a bath is not available, until the limb becomes warm.
And in a gash made along the side of the vein by cutting either lengthwise or behind it you should use the scalpel, or at its base you should use the cautery.
And when you open it with hooks and scrape the vein from all sides until it appears to the touch, and you should notice its appearance declining to blackness.
And thus when you expose it and from the wound you see that white one which is the membrane.
And then you place the ligature below where it is elevated and turned from the wound, and suspend it with hooks for one hour.
And then make another incision near that incision at three points, and then scrape the wound above the vein until it appears.
And then elevate that and incise it at the rear incision using the cautery, and then extract that and elevate it until it comes out through the second incision, and then extract that from the incision above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or farthest from all the incisions, until everything comes out; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you to extract and elevate it, then insert a needle with a strong double thread and tie it and extract it, or insert below the ligature and move your hand in all directions until it is dislodged. And be careful that it is not cut; for if it is broken, it will be difficult for you to elevate it, and the harm will have come about because of this injury.
And when all that is done, place over the site of the wound a piece of wool soaked in wine and rose oil and plain oil, and care for it until it is healed.
And if the vessel is contracted with tortuosities to which there is an envelope on the parts, and it does not follow straightness as we have said, then you should cut along each part of the tortuosity and at the places where the envelope is, and then lift it up by hooks until you elevate the whole.
And be careful in your section that you do not incise the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; use your prudence in this.
The shape of the incision for cutting the vessel:
It is not sharpened like hooks nor has a sharp tip so as not to injure the vein; and it should be of thick flexibility like a lion's, for if it were fine, it would cut the vein because of its fineness; however, it should be in excess of thickness as we have said.

Of the incision above worms generated under the skin and it is called bovine illness.

On incision for the disease nafir.
This illness is called nafir in our homeland, and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, and it often changes from one limb to another.
And I will now describe that illness as I shall recount it to you. I was called to attend to a sick woman in a village, and when I uncovered her arm, I saw a small inflammation in the funis vein of the arm. After it had been ruptured for about an hour, I observed that this inflammation spread as quickly as wax would melt and rise up, but more rapidly than could be expected, like living silver flowing from place to place.
And then the pain moved from that location to the muscle, and it stayed there for about an hour.
And then it spread to the other parts of the body until it reached the other arm.
And then she told me that it was moving throughout her body as I had observed, and I was greatly amazed by the transformation and the speed at which it moved from one limb to another. And I had seen something similar before in my own patients in that manner, but I have never seen the pain transform from one limb to another as I did then. But I have not seen anything like this in my other patients as I did with the woman.
And I thought that this was due to the fact that the woman was of advanced age, with multiple ailments and exposed veins, and in such conditions, one might experience a transformation of sensations.
It is necessary that there does not appear according to this retroversion in those who are delicate and whose flesh is thin and the veins prominent.
And when you want to perform the treatment on it, and the patient feels that pain, at that moment do not proceed as we have said, but instead press strongly on it and evacuate that trapped air until it is released, and then cauterize the area.
But if you do not perform it with your hands, then treat that by inducing vomiting and expelling the air, and also use preparations to remove foul pills and senna pills and similar remedies.
Of the extraction of splinters.
Splinters vary according to their types and the parts of the body where they lodge. Their diversity is such that some have large points, others small points, some are concave, and others blunt; some are solid, while others have three angles; there are also those with tongues or star-like shapes.
And the signs of concussion are when it strikes in the sagitta, and penetrates the bone, and exits through the sinciput which is above the head, because there occur soda vehement, and scotomia, and vertigo, and rigidity in the eyes, and inflammation and rigidity of the tongue, and spasms and mixture of understanding and vomiting of bile, and it easily severs speech and destroys the voice, and exits from the place of the injury moisture white like dust, and there appears on the face an aquosity of the flesh.
And so if these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it strikes in the heart, so that it is near the left nipple, and feels as if it were fixed in a hard place, not in a soft one; and very often the movement of the arrow is like the movement of a pulse, and there flows from the wound black blood, and chills those extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting; and thus you know that death will come soon.
And the signs when the arrow has pierced the lung are that foam of blood comes from the wound, and the veins near the neck become swollen, and the complexion changes, and he exhales a great sigh, and seeks to draw in cold air.
And if the arrow strikes in the diaphragm which is in the chest, and the pain then is near the small ribs, and it causes great and violent pain and sighing, and one cannot move any of the body's muscles.
And if the arrow strikes in the stomach, then very often food that is undigested appears at the wound.
And if the arrow is embedded in the belly, and it is fixed within it, make some incision in the wound, or the membrane, or the vessel which is ruptured, and there is no hope for the cure without extracting the arrow.
And if the arrow is embedded in the bladder, and urine comes out from it, and the process occurs due to some cause affecting the parts outside, and pain becomes severe over the injury, then you know that it is fatal.
And the other members as well, such as the face, the neck, and the shoulder, and the shoulders and the helper muscles, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the ribs and similar parts of the limbs, are saved according to the best dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a venomous arrow.
And I recount this to you as I have observed about the nature of these arrows, so that you may understand through this about your own treatment.
The reason for this is that in a person who was hit by an arrow at the lacrimal bone near the root of the nose, I removed it from the other side, left the gray matter of the ear alone, and he healed without any damage to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had struck him in the gray matter of his eye, leaving the eyelid intact. The arrow was deeply embedded and could not be completely removed; only a small part of it remained attached to its shaft. It was a large sharp arrow of the Turks, made of iron like a lion's claw, which did not have feathers.
And the Jew recovered, and there was no adverse effect on his eye.
About extracting one arrow from a Christian man who was struck in the groin, and it was an Arabic arrow, and it had feathers on it; then I incised between two grooves, and it was submerged in his flesh there; then I carefully removed that, and the Christian man recovered.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who was struck in his abdomen, and I thought he would die from it; but after thirty days had passed and there was no change in his condition for the worse, I incised around the arrow and engaged with it, and he recovered, and no further harm came to him.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow was embedded between two parts of his body, and the wound had healed over it, and after seven years had passed, the arrow emerged at the root of the injury.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow was embedded in her abdomen, and the wound had healed over it, and there was no change in her condition for the worse, and the woman did not suffer any harm from any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow was embedded in his face, and the wound had healed over it, and he broke the arrow without feeling any pain, and many similar cases are like this.

And he plastered the place with an adhesive and digestive plaster, persuading me to put the place where the abscess appeared by him on the arrow wound, so that it might dry over it.
And no significant accident occurred at the site where the arrow had reached; and I continued with the plaster on it for several days without any incident; and as the wound healed, after some days' time, one day he felt the arrow within his abdomen and reported this to me.

And soon I adjusted the appearance of the wound with that acute medicine until the tip of the arrow became visible to me, and it took me four months for this to happen.

On phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy of which it is customary to perform phlebotomy as follows: xxx, in the head sixteen, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called alhasisain; two veins visible on each temple; and two veins that are at the corners of the eyes, called aspicientz; one vein located in the middle of the forehead, and a vein at the tip of the nose; two veins on the neck; two veins on the upper lip; and two veins on the lower lip; and — these are the veins called algelic — ; and there are also two veins below the tongue. The veins that are phlebotomized in the arms number five. Of these, one is a cephalic vein, which they call the wild latus; and people refer to it as "the head vein," and it is also called a median vein, composed of a branch from the basilica and another branch from the cephalic; and people name this "the vein of the whole body." The basilica vein lies on the domestic arm and is named "citillar"; common people call it "the belly vein." Another is the funis of the arm, which lies above the elbow, in which phlebotomy is performed, and it appears prominently over the wrist. And there is also the salvatella vein, which is located between the thumb and the little finger, appearing on both sides.
And on the back and in the groin there are three veins; one of them is that which is behind the curvature of the rib cage; and the second is sophena, and its place is behind the groin of the domestic animal; and the sciatic vein, and its place is behind the groin of the wild animal; and in the groin there are other similar veins.
The treatment of the veins that are near the ears is for catarrhs, migraines, fevers, and old ulcers of the head.
And the quality of treating those veins is as I recommend to you: shave the head of the patient, and rub the head of the patient in the occipital region at the place where both veins are with a large piece of cloth for good friction; then tighten the neck of the patient with a bandage so that the two veins appear near the ears in two places on the back of the head; and feel those veins with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those veins with your finger, here is where you should proceed.
Then take the lancet, and it should be sharp, and then insert it behind the vein into the tendon until the blood flows to the bone. And then raise your hand with the vein and the tendon towards the upper parts and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be the quantity of two fingers, adjusted or around that amount; and let as much blood out as you wish.
And then apply pressure with powders and keep it compressed until it is healed.

First Chapter.
An overview and collection from the treatment of fractures of bones, including those whose preliminary steps are necessary before we begin to recall the details of each type of fracture and their various treatments. Therefore, let us say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the speech; and the chapters to which understanding is necessary at the beginning for the practitioner to stand on the truth of those; so that you and others who know or will learn this noble art may understand.
And therefore I say that when it happens to someone to suffer a fracture, dislocation, torsion, or fall, at that moment you should consider at the beginning whether to perform bloodletting or to treat them otherwise, along with whatever else is appropriate, unless some cause prohibits it, such as weakness of strength, or if it affects an infant, or an elderly person in decrepitude, or during times of extreme heat or cold.
And accordingly, be content with food that is light, such as fresh herbs and the meat of birds and small animals; and avoid heavy meats and foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are sure of a safe recovery from inflammation, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter into the limb.
Then return the patient to their usual regimen when they are accustomed to it.
And when the broken bone begins to be restored, it is then necessary for the patient to be fed nutritious foods that are moist and heavy, such as those made from wheat boiled with water, rice, peas, beef tripe, eggs, fresh meat, and similar items. And with this regimen, coagulation of the fracture occurs more easily and better.
And know that bones fractured in humans during the first fever and in the elderly cannot be continued and consolidated according to their nature by the drying of the limbs.
But nature adjusts and applies them around the broken bone from all parts without any cause similar to glue, in which there is a thickness that adheres and strengthens it as they conjoin and unite, until they reach their full strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning, without any cause hindering them from performing their works.
And for this reason, it is necessary to withhold from the patient foods that are heavy and viscous and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the thigh is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other members are different among themselves.
And you will find a description of every type of fracture, expanded in its own separate chapter.
Note the diversity of fractures within themselves. And the types of bone fractures are diverse within themselves; for one kind of arch-shaped fracture is different from those that occur in the stars; and some fractures are according to the length of the bone: so the fractures of the shaft, splinters, and open and closed fractures proceed as when a pen is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture due to a blow and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And each type has its own proper treatment in the restoration of it, as there will be a separate description of them in their respective places.
Note the signs of a bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: its twisting, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and how it feels when you press on it.
And therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the place or sound, and when you touch the bone with movement and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there may be twisting or a small fissure; therefore, you should not press on it with your hand and compress it entirely. Instead, pause and apply the medicines of which there will be a reminder soon at the moment when it is appropriate to do so; and note that it might be tight due to subtle constriction.
And know that when the place is arched and not split into two or another part with it without being affected by swelling, as it remains whole from its beginning, then you should begin from your time to correct the swelling that affects the abscess.
And if an abscess affects it, then leave that for some days until the abscess subsides; and afterward bandage that according to each method you can by lightness and ease.
And know that the restoration of it and adjustment of it is easier than that of the bone in which swelling occurs.
And then strengthen that according to what will come to mind regarding it.
But if swelling occurs in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from both parts, whether it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or one cord and your hand.
And place yourself in such a position that you can put the limb down bound according to its natural figure, while the parts of the broken member are extended, then it forces you to return those addictions with ease and according to each way you can back to their places.
And be careful according to your strength that no harm comes to the patient due to your operation or injury; and strive according to your ability that you apply one bone to its companion bone according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that opportune moment you touch with your hand that which needs attention, and if you see any different cause, correct that and bind it according to the extent of your skill.
And from an incorrect extension and compression, so do false doctors with their foolish manipulations cause an abscess to occur by their operation, or they cause a contraction in the limb as we have seen this happen through their foolish manipulations.
And the quality of the constriction of the tranquil limb is what I recommend; for you to know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and greatness and in the form of those.
And for those that are small like the arm and the finger and the wrist and similar to these, then the wrapping should be of fine soft cloth.
But for those that are large like the thigh or the chest, it is necessary to wrap them with linen and hard material, because the wrapping of linen connects the large limb and strengthens it uniformly in constriction, ensuring that no dirt enters.
And after beginning, after your equalization evacuation, pause appropriately for this, on the site of that fracture with three wraps or four depending on the limb; and tighten it gently with the bandage.
And apply it to the part where the main pus comes from the place of the fracture, and tighten it so much that you do not loosen the compression with which you have tightened the place of the fracture. And apply an extension to the wrapping of the fracture site slightly, and loosen the compression little by little until you have removed a large part of it.
And then take another bandage and wrap the fractured site again with it, and then apply to the lower pus part of the wrapping; and your procedure should be in the compression of the wrapping and the loosening of it as we have said about the first upper wrapping.
And then pause between the wrappings with a piece of linen or cloth on which the curvature of the fracture is modeled, if there is any curvature; and if not, do not place anything in it for this reason.
And then place over the other bandage, and then apply plasters properly made right away if there is no inflammation or abscess in the area, and at that time place over what causes the abscess and reduces the inflammation; and leave this for several days; and then you tighten over it the bandages.
And let the plasters be about half a cubit wide and well made wisely; or let them be of fir wood; or of palm leaves; and let the width of each plaster be according to this form and this figure:
However, the plaster that is placed over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others, and the length of the plaster should match the size of the limb; then wrap over the plasters with another bandage according to your initial compression; and then bind it above with fine linen as we have said about compression, and this should be your compression according to the location of the fracture, and the further away from the fracture, the less should be the compression.
And let the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and let them be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some foolish people use ropes made from twisted fibers for their threads, this is a great error, when one sees the stitches uneven.
And likewise, fine threads are not suitable, for you will not achieve with stitches what you desire; and it is necessary that there be more than a finger's width between stitch and stitch.
And if the patient is in pain at the ends of the bones near the stitches in those places, then pause and use bandages made of lion's wool or crimson wool until it does not cause injury to them.

And know that all broken limbs are not set with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that when the limb is large, it should not be placed on splints immediately but after about five or seven days, or more, according to your certainty of the occurrence of pus.

Method of making a plaster for the rapid healing of fractures, suitable for various conditions and particularly for women and children, as it does not produce heat or irritation: take powdered flour, which is the fine residue that sticks to the walls of the mill during grinding.

Chapter Second: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct. Some fractures are caused by the impact of a hard object, and the impact dislodges all the bone inward toward the brain, which is behind the bone, just as it would in wood; therefore, this type is called an indentation. Or the object may cut some part of the bone and cut through the flesh but does not penetrate to the backside of it; and this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the severity of these two fractures can be either great or slight, and there is also a fracture called comminuted or crushed; the cause of this is an impact with something weak or falling onto a stone, and similar circumstances.
And this fracture is penetrating that approaches the panniculus, which is below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the danger of these two fractures is great or little.
And of the fracture that is weakened in the bone, in the subtlety of the small threads, and for this it is named this particular kind of fracture capillary.
And it is a fracture that is made either by falling or by the impact of a stone and similar things, which causes some lamina of bone to enter into the soft parts inside, and it occurs in the concave place just as it happens with bones when they suffer an impact and break upon entering the soft parts.
And this happens more often in the bones of human bodies, just as it does with children's skulls.
And for all these kinds of fractures, treatments are applied, which are pieces just like small stars separated. And I will return to the recapitulation of all this in its place.
And you should recognize all these kinds of fractures by detection on them and by inquiry into their attempts and the state of the flesh that is corrupted over them.
And the capillary kind is recognized by this: it appears on the bone, and when it is cleaned, it shows as a line on the bone because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider at the beginning the symptoms of the patient, and if you see from these symptoms that they signify by clear indication extreme fear, such as vomiting like bile, extension, loss of understanding, hoarseness, fainting, acute fever, and similar symptoms with their severity, doubt whether the patient will be healed.
And if for uncommon accidents you hope for a cure, then you begin the treatment of them.
Which cause is four if it has come to you the illness at the beginning as it is an illness and this is in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you study in the condition of the bone before the day fourteen according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you wait to remove the bone before the seventh day, knowing that what is around the bone may become corrupted by inflammation, and that accidents which we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain and is in small pieces and adhered, then it is necessary that you incise the fragmented and adhered part as I shall recount to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the sick person, and expose the bone according to custom and method as best you can according to the shape of the injury, so that it is clear on the patient.
And if it happens to you that you need to detect the bone, or there is bleeding or an abscess requiring cautery, then you must proceed with what is necessary, and it is that you apply the area with cloths infused in wine and olive oil, and allow the wound to rest until the abscess is subdued, and you are sure of no further bleeding; then begin the circular incision around the bone and its removal.
And this should be according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with an incisor of narrow shape, and this is the form of it: And then apply another incisor to it, slightly wider than the previous one; and this is the form of it:
Then apply the lancet to cut out the pus that remains.
And it is necessary that you have various lancets, some of which are used for cutting out pus deeper than others; and some are sharper at their tips than the rest, and they are made of iron or leather.
And use them with ease in tapping on the lancets so that you do not injure the head and skin of the patient.
And if the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to perforate around it before you apply the lancets, which are called shallow forceps.
And they are called this because these do not reach the depth of the bone beneath them. For the forceps have a rounded tip or margin above that which is near the head, similar to a small border or circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
It is convenient that you have many of these forceps, each of which should suit the sharpness of different bones. Ensure that at hand for every part of the brain, the tip of which is sharp, and the quantity in length and breadth is according to the size and sharpness of this bone.
This is the method for three types of forceps: The quality of perforating around the broken bone is that you place the forceps on the bone and rotate them with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the forceps to another location, doing this throughout the extent equal to its thickness or thereabouts.
Here is the translation:
And see that you keep such carefulness that the incisor does not touch any part of the thigh.
And thus when you have circled the bone to perforate it, and it is separated from the thigh if it is continuous with it, and you will extract that, see that you rasp and equalize that in the roughness of it with something that is in the bone, but however see that it is more thin and finer than other incisors.
And if any part of the small and delicate bones remains, now take that with ease with what is suitable to you among the instruments; and then treat the wound with lint and dressings as we have said.
And the other method of operation is light, distant from fear, which Guy of Chauliac and many great authors praise; and this is the speech about it: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until when you have uncovered that part and you have paused below it with the tip of this slow and cheerful incisor, which has this form:
And it is that the lens-shaped part remains intact and does not cut any area, while the sharp part divides into two sides along its length, as you see; that the lens-shaped part be fitted to the thigh with the sharp face of it against the bone.
And then strike upon the incisor of one part with a small hammer until you cut all the bone with ease as it is revolved, and be sure about the soft tissue that no accident happens in any way, and even if the surgeon were the most inexperienced of all men and the least knowledgeable among them; certainly, and even if he were asleep.
If there is any reason to cut into the tissue beneath the skin in certain bone locations, insert the pointed part of the knife-like instrument and deliver it where it is easy to do so, for it should be done without harm or fear.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate into the tissue beneath the skin, and what is abraded or remains of the bone is its surface, and it lies in roughness and small protrusions, then you should scrape that roughness and eradicate those protrusions with fine razors which you have prepared in various quantities so that you can administer them appropriately at any location during your operation on the broken bone and according to its shape.
And you should administer at your ratio with which you scrape the bone at the beginning with the broader of those razors, and gradually use one finer than the other, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach administering the thinnest and finest of all of them.
But in each of the other small and delicate fractures and subtle fractures, you should administer care according to what is needed for the correction of them.
And it is because they are not afraid about all those who have studied in that art. And stand on what we have written regarding major fractures.
And if the tissue beneath the skin is intact when you remove the bone, then take a piece of cloth according to the size of the wound, and dip it in warm oil, and place that on the wound opening; and then take another folded or triple-layered cloth and dip it in wine and warm oil, and place that on the first cloth; do this as gently as possible so as not to injure the tissue around the wound. Then apply a broad bandage over it, but not too tightly, just enough to cover the wound.
If you are certain that this condition requires treatment in one day or two before you are sure of an abscess being cauterized; and then untie that and immediately administer medicines which counter excessive dryness, such as lint boiled in water and flour of wheat, and powder of endive and arishta, and similar to those.
And it is that you should make of these medicines powder which is finely ground over the wound, as they are dry; and generally, administer to it all medicine whose property is to cleanse without irritating.
And be careful according to your virtue that the wound in your treatment is clean; and let it not become infected or inflamed; nor allow it to increase in virulence. For when it is placed over the panniculus cerebri, it corrupts that part and weakens it, and you will bring upon the patient that malignant malice.
And it affects the brain, as when the bone is penetrated and especially when the treatment of it is compressed, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore consider these signs if you see in the patient the accidents we have mentioned, know that it is dangerous without any doubt.
And if the blackness does not come from the medicine placed above it but is due to the nature of that medicine, then take one part honey and three parts olive oil, and soak a piece of cloth in this mixture, and place it over the wound; and subsequently treat it with the spices suitable for healing until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is cut.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two main places, or sometimes one of those lesser areas, because there you have two bones, since the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken, but only contusion and swelling and bruising occurs there.
If one of the two main parts is broken, then you should insert your finger into the side of the nose, and equalize that fracture with your index finger inside and your thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and your finger cannot reach it, then you should equalize it at the end of a smooth rod which is small in diameter.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should proceed as described.
And when you restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and otherwise, then around the seventh or tenth day, you should rest from cauterizing the abscess.
And then insert into the end of the nose a piece of linen cloth if the fracture is in one part, or place two bandages if the fracture is in two parts, and let the bandage be thick enough to fill the hollow of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the bandages should be soaked in butter, and changed every day, but I have not seen this; instead, let the cloth be soaked in egg white mixed with powdered flour; then leave it on until the bone is firm and the cartilage has hardened.
In some cases, place a poultice on the nose instead of cauterization, as it is wrapped in cloth from a lion; and if the fracture of the nose is too severe and the patient is prohibited from inspiration.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do that, and if you want, do cauterizations.
And if another poultice is applied after cauterization, apply a plaster to the nose with pitch or with wax infused in vinegar and rose oil, or with cerate.
But if no poultice is applied after cauterization, now you should apply plasters to that with sesame flour and powdered coriander seeds mixed with egg white; and then place it over the lion's paw, and do not put anything on the nose.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, now you should cut around them, and extract them with instruments as is appropriate; and then close the fissure, and treat it with what promotes healing and consolidation, and with plasters suitable for that.
And if there is swelling inside the nose, now you should treat it with cauterization and administer lead tubes until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no swelling around it, now check if the fracture is only on the surface and not split into two parts and curved inward, then its diagnosis is easy.
If the fracture is on the right side, place your left thumb in the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, use your right thumb; then push the bone fragments back into their proper position with ease using your other hand from the outer part; proceed gently but effectively.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two parts, then you should apply extension to both parts as much as possible to achieve proper alignment.
And if there is a concussion of the teeth or disruption, then strengthen those that you expect will break with thread of gold or silver or silk; and immediately apply cerate to the fractured jaw, and then place it on doubled cloth, and place over it a large piece of linen properly made, or a pad of wax equal in length to the jaw; and then bind it above as is appropriate for your bandaging method and as you find convenient, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food in the form of thin soups.
And if you find that the figure is already altered in any way, then you should change and loosen it on the third day; and then correct what has been altered of it and plaster it with powder of linseed, egg white, or flour of chickpeas, after you have removed the cerate from it, and place over the plaster thin wool.
And then, as it is now attached and adjusted the plaster over it, and if the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture is strong; for usually this fracture becomes strong in three weeks.
And if another abscess occurs that needs to be cauterized, then administer what we have said about similar cases in the section on reducing inflammation, and remove that abscess.
And if the fracture is a splinter, then consider whether the bone fragment is separated or displaced. At this point, use your skill to remove those fragments with what is appropriate for you in their removal with the instruments available.
And if the opening of the splinter is narrow, then enlarge it with a scalpel as much as your need requires.
And when you have removed those fragments, and if they cannot be completely extracted, then close the opening of the splinter if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the plasters that are appropriate for this condition to promote healing until it is healed.
Chapter 5
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
There are various ways the collarbone can be fractured in front of the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three manners.
For it may be broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without any splinters being involved, and this is easier for its restoration; or it may involve splinters, making its restoration difficult; or the fracture may be accompanied by a splinter.
And the procedure in such a case, when the fracture is accompanied by a splinter, is that you have two assistants present: one to support and guide the broken collarbone, and the other to extend the neck toward that area.
In adjusting the fracture align the bones so that it takes on the form most appropriate to what is necessary, and do not leave any prominence or depression.
And if you need more extension, now it is necessary to place under the armpit of the injured side a ball made of cloth or wool, and let its size be according to the quantity needed; extend and elevate the shoulder and compress the ball with your hand so that you align the fracture as required.
And if you cannot pull the shoulder extremity outwards towards the side, so that it reaches the proper position, now it is necessary for the injured person to lie supine on their neck, and place something under the muscle of one side, about midway in size.
And the assistant presses the muscle of it towards the rear until the bone of the shoulder is elevated to its proper position, and then you correct the fracture and align it with the bones as described.
But if you feel hard knots of the shoulder and pain, now it is necessary to incise over those areas and extract those knots with ease.
And if it is stuck in the bone, now you must devise an incision with one of the instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, since you have placed under the shoulder the tool used by the surgeon; and this tool is made of wood or iron and has the following form:
And it is similar to a bowl whose interior is concave; and its width should be according to what you need from it, depending on the size of the bone or its smallness.
Make the length of the instrument as long as is possible for the operation; let it have two ends just as you please, and one end be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure it will form an abscess, then you should now approximate the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear it will form an abscess soon, then you should employ the wound with dressings and powders according to the quantity of the wound's disruption.
And if an abscess forms soon, then apply a dressing with vinegar and rose oil, and place it over that.
And thus, do not let the splint rest on the broken bone; and therefore it is necessary that you see the patient every day, so when the bandage has loosened or you see the splint already moved, then you must correct this and tighten the bandaging.
And place the patient's pillow on their neck, and place underneath it a small roll to raise the head during sleep; therefore, in this way, the support of the chest will be raised from that side, and thus the fracture of the collarbone is elevated according to the muscle; or tie the arm to the neck.
And do not remove the bandage unless you find an abscess in the area within twelve days.
And then change the plaster if you see the face of it like that, and adjust it in the constriction and restore it until it is set and the collarbone fracture is healed.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the method in twenty-eight days, and in some cases in less time for some people.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The scapula is broken in the middle and not fractured at the edges but only the margins.
And when it is broken at that location — and it is broken in the middle part of it — and this is known only by palpation, then according to the shape of the fracture prepare yourself and reduce it according to its natural figure in every way you can manage.
And place over it powdered alum of hauv and lion's fat; and next place cotton wool doubled; and then place upon the lint cloth, fine powder according to the quantity of the spoon all or at most a little.
And if the lint cloth is concave in some parts of the spoon, then you fill that concavity with lion's fat, and then press the lint cloth according to equality.
And then wrap it above with linen strips with appropriate compression and fix the lint cloth in place so that it does not move from its position.
And examine the bandaging each day; and whenever it needs to be softened, change it and wrap it again, and adjust the lint cloth if it has moved from its position.
And place the dressing on its side as appropriate.
And the spoon is restored in xx days or in .xxv.
And when the number is complete, then you undo the bandage, and you know for certain that this is of the bones of which the beginning was not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone piece and place the bandage below it, then you cut above it, remove it, and do as we have said in the fracture of the collarbone for the pus formation or cauterization if this occurs here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of a foot fracture.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle and remains there; but the extremities move, which causes cicatrisation and comminution.
And of the accidents of it when the middle of it is broken is that it inclines to the internal parts and there occurs great pain, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and heavy bleeding; and it is declared in the cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies supine on their back, and a bolster is placed between their shoulders; and gently press the muscles of it, and adjust the ribs by more than two ribs, and carefully do the operation and equalization in every possible way and suitable for this, until the figure of the bone returns as needed.
And then place on it a plaster and cotton, and lay over fine sticks of willow or hazel or similar in lightness, after which you wrap that in cloth; and then gently bind that over the broken bone so that it is not too tight; and proceed with bandaging according to the roundness of the back several times, and tighten that moderately; and then consider the bandage at all times, and when it has loosened, tighten that again.
And if necessity compels the removal of that which has been bound by corrosion or pus or an abscess that has occurred in the area, then you should cut and untie it and remove the plaster and correct what has happened according to this method of treatment; and immediately replace the plaster if you have done it to the face; and adjust the bandaging until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in thick parts where they join; and at their extremities in front, such contusion does not happen to them because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the feeling of those who examine it with the said methods.
And the restoration should be such that it aligns the fracture with the said methods in the possible manner, until it is set as needed, and then apply plaster and bind the broken bone with a splint if necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the inner parts, then severe and piercing pain occurs, like the pain that happens to one who has pleurisy, and because the bone punctures the membrane; and difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood occur; and this is difficult to treat.
And the Ancients were skilled in that with powerful remedies.
And what they say is: it is necessary to avoid foods that produce inflammation and gas, so that the abdomen does not become swollen and extended, and thus the fracture is pushed outward.
Let us proceed with that which will not be a cause of generating an abscess by burning, if it does not occur; and if it has already occurred, now aid in that.
And some of them say that it is paused above the wound and close to where it is drawn with strength; and this is similar to reasoning, but it is feared that the wound may draw the superfluities to the place due to weakness.
And some of them say: cover the place with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and place powders between the folds until it is full, so that the bandage is applied as if wrapping it according to its roundness; and near it, treat the injury with the treatment for pleurisy using food and medicine.
And if the cause of the injury is violent and cannot be endured, and the bone pierces through the membrane with a point, we fear for the injury; now it should be that we cut over the place, uncover the broken edge, and then apply an instrument to secure the stability thereof, the memory of which precedes, and that they saw the bone with ease, and extract it.
And then we adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs internally, now you should incise it and apply powder in roasted oil on the place, and treat the injury with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And he should lie on the side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the second vertebra and the neck.
In some cases, there is a fracture of the neck bones, and it takes time for them to heal, similarly with the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will recover or not, at that moment consider whether their skin is already softened, stunned, deadened, unable to move them or stretch them, and when you press on them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and they do not feel pain in them, then you know that they are not recovering, and in such conditions it is fatal.
And if you press on them and they feel constriction from the pressure and pain when pricked with a needle, then you know that the vertebra is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if the vertebrae of the neck show signs similar to this and you want to know if they will recover or not, then at that moment consider their breathing.
If you see that they are already weakened and the same thing happens to them as described for the skin, and shortly after lying on their back, they experience vomiting and involuntary diarrhea, and in some cases they lie on their back and want to urinate but cannot, then you know it is fatal, and at that point there is no need to work on their treatment.
But if none of those symptoms occur, the cause is better and farther removed from danger.
And the treatment for them is to apply a poultice to reduce the swelling on the affected vertebra using raw oil or boiled linseed; place this poultice on it several times a day until the swelling subsides.
During the hours of rest, apply one of the comforting and healing plasters, and stretch over that bandage; and command the patient to use rest and calmness and not to sleep on the side where pain is felt until it is healed.
And if it happens that the bone is bruised or has separated for any reason, then you should set it in place and hold that bone; and immediately bring together the two edges of the wound, with stitches if it is large; and then treat it with some flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
But if the tip of the bone is fractured at the end, and it is the joint, then you should insert your finger into the backside, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second-degree adjustment; and then apply a plaster and splint to it, if you need those, and then secure it.
And if you feel bruised and fractured bones there, then act upon that, remove them, and treat the wound with the treatment that is prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the broad (likely referring to the treatment of wide wounds or injuries).
The bones of the broad areas are often fractured, and when they are fractured, then their fracture is nothing but a communication at the extremities of those bones and they are split along their length and deviated to the sides; and the patient experiences pain in that location and puncture, and the limb is paralyzed when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it aligns with the figure of the fracture.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of the limb, then adjust it as seems appropriate to you by equalizing it so that it resembles its natural shape.
And if the fracture is in the bone that already inclines downward, then have the patient lie on their stomach so that you can prepare the restoration of that fracture. And when you have set it properly, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or cane beside it, and bind it tightly with bandages so that there is no risk of the fracture being moved or the splint becoming loose.
And set the concavity by filling it so that it takes on an even shape, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or side.
And if an abscess occurs suddenly, then stop the extension and restoration of it until the abscess is brought under control, and apply what is appropriate to sedate the abscess as has been previously described; and then return to restoring it, and bind it as is necessary.
And if the bones are displaced or crushed, then you must treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the adjunct bone.
The adjunct is what lies between the spine and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is fractured, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, one of which involves taking a piece of wood of medium thickness in this shape:
Let the ends of two bandages be held, and let it be suspended from a high place.
Follow the malalignment on a stool; and then lay the broken arm out over the wood until it reaches the midpoint where the curve of the wood matches the curvature of the arm.
And then suspend something heavy from it, so that the patient extends it downward with their hands; and then the physician should place the fracture along with its parts above in such a way that the fracture returns to its proper position.
And another method is that the patient lies supine on their neck and suspend the hand of the neck with bandages; and then instruct two assistants, one to hold what is above the fracture along with its parts, and the other to hold below the patient; and each should extend it in their respective direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then pull on the fracture with bandages and on that with another set of bandages, and each of the two assistants should extend in their respective directions.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary to place half of the bandage over the midpoint and the other part below the fracture near the joint.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then it is necessary to place the bandage over that location and around that joint.
Similarly, do this with ease and effort, and then when it is properly adjusted as needed and applied well, pull on that if an abscess does not occur, using caution.
If an abscess is to be cauterized, if there is no stricture within seven days, and you pause upon the wool bandage soaked in vinegar for six times and rose oil so that the abscess may rest, then tighten it.
And the method of tightening that is to place a plaster on the fracture, and then wrap it with a wet cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that auxiliary, and place its hand on the side muscle.
And wrap the cloth and tighten around the auxiliary for the arm to be in a place of stability, if there is no cause from the broken bone that prohibits this.
And now use splints.
And it is that you place on the fracture that splint which is thicker and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break plus three fingers from both ends.
And then tighten around the splints with the bandage as I have described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandaging over the site of the fracture should be tighter. And the farther apart the ends of the fracture are, the looser the bandaging should be.
And thus, when you position the splints and bandaging as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then do so.
And if you fear an abscess forming, then leave the bandaging and splints in place for seven days as we have said.
And therefore consider the bandage in all parts for three days so that no pus or inflammation occurs; or it is prohibited for food to reach the member due to excess or constriction; and thus correct everything as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not loosen the ligature for at least three days or more.
And let the patient lie on their back, and place pillows under their head so that it is raised higher than the stomach.
And place a pad filled with wool evenly on the injured area, and at all times of the night or day, ensure that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, nor should the bandage be too loose; thus correct this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned earlier, such that the food is light at the beginning until healing starts; and then gradually increase the quantity of food.
For the usual practice is that the bandage and splint remain tight for forty days; and then they should be loosened, a bath given, and medicines applied with those that correct this.
And if the fracture is rough and uneven, then do not loosen the bandage or remove the splints for one day or even up to two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna and radius; one is small and lies under the thumb, while the other is larger and lies beneath the smaller bone, closer to the lower part.
If only the larger bone or the smaller one is broken, and if they are broken in such a way.
And when the smaller bone is broken above, the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the bone is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when two bones are broken at once.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller one above, it is appropriate for the physician, due to its easy restoration, to allow a short period of extension with ease until it is set.
But if the larger bone is the one that is broken, then a more vigorous extension should be allowed.
And if both bones are broken at once, then it is necessary to allow for the most vigorous extension.
And it is necessary to use the figure of the hand for its restoration and extension stretched out on a board, with the wrist at the top end, higher than all the other fingers, and the thumb lower than the others, and the patient lying on a sloping bed.
And there should be a board in front of him at an appropriate height or straight ahead, so that the patient does not have to strain.
And the attendant should extend the arm from below with the patient's hand or with a splint, and another attendant should extend it from above similarly.
And the surgeon should then place the bone in such a way that it regenerates in the best possible shape.
And if the bone is fractured, you should then reduce all parts to their proper place with your skill.
And if a separate fragment appears that you cannot join and it is not within your hope to restore it, then act on it as we have described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by an infection, I have already dedicated a separate chapter to that, so follow the treatment described there.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with cerote made of rose oil and white wax, which is midway between thickness and subtlety, and apply it over the area where the abscess is present in a fine layer; then remove the cerote and place on an emplaster prepared from powdered myrrh mixed with clear honey, and afterwards apply plasters; let the plaster placed over that fracture be more distant and firmer.
And know that the number of plasters for the arm varies depending on the major disposition, whether the fracture is in one fossa or in two adjacent fossae.
And then apply your bandage over the site of the fracture more firmly and vigorously, and when it is most suitable to place pressure on the parts above or below the fracture, apply the bandage slightly looser as previously mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough, and the thread with which they are wrapped should be of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the member and the bandage for some days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, as promptly as possible deal with the condition affecting the member, and then immerse the member in hot water until that condition subsides.
And leave the member undisturbed until it rests, and then resume the bandaging.
And if the bandaging has already been loosened and the bone is already set and appears to be in a similar condition, then adjust everything with your skill.
And consider whether the nutrition is prevented from reaching the member due to excessive bandaging, for then you should loosen it slightly and leave it for some days until nutrition reaches that area, and then strengthen it.
But if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to remove the bandage for about 20 days or thereabouts.
And then have the patient's arm suspended by their own skill in making agitated movements, and make them lie down to sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in about 30 days or 32 days, or sometimes it is restored in 28 days, and all of this depends on the condition of the tissues and the skill involved.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and of the other fingers.
Know that the thumb and the joints of the other fingers quickly heal from fractures, and they do not suffer any significant complications.
When it happens that the bone is fractured or dislocated, at that moment it is necessary for the patient to lie flat and facing upwards according to equality, and to place the arm on top of that extended.
And then the surgeon should extend the broken bones and the physician should align them so that they heal properly.
Now it is necessary to apply a plaster and cotton, unless an abscess forms in which case cauterize; then place a splint on top according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that with cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, then make it appear as a ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold that with their fractured hand; then bind it tightly with cloth.
And let the splint be placed against the skin with cloth in which it is elevated, so that the splint adheres to the palm; and bind it as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the parts outside, then it is necessary to place a splint above and another splint below on the palm, so that the hand is supported and straight; and then bind it, as if rotating the hand at the wrist with bandages.
And if there is a fracture in one of the bones of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, align it as necessary and then bind it to the palm; and if you wish, place a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not worsened.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, index finger, ring finger, or little finger, then align it and bind it to the affected finger; or bind all of them continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on that finger, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and approach that, the disposition of the abscess both heated and opposed — according to the nature of the operation — with what is needed when some cause of those occurs, as it is repeated in the narration.
Chapter XIV.
On the restoration of a fracture of the collarbone.
The collarbone is often fractured and is made apparent by sensation according to how it has shifted both forward and upward.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that one bandage is stretched over the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient lies prone on their belly and face.
And the practitioner should extend all the bandages to their appropriate places according to their quality when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is closer to the shoulder joint, then the bandage should be placed at an angle towards the part of the armpit, so that the extension stops there, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is closer to the armpit, then the bandage should be placed towards the armpit, and the extension should go to the parts below.
And then the physician equalizes the bone with both of his hands so that it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by proper union; now it is necessary to apply plaster and bandaging, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess does occur, then you should leave it alone for several days until the heated abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The treatment of the bandage should be done as follows: wrap around the fracture a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and trim off the excess; then wrap the joint so that it reaches to the thigh and the root of the spine.
And insert the bandage between the bone and the joint near the end towards the lower parts, and let the ends of the bandage reach to the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the joint and the bone what is left of the bandage.
And then place over the bone at the location of the fracture splints, and place one strip over the bone of the joint.
And then fill the spaces between the joint and the bone with soft cloths, so that the bandaging is compacted.
And then begin the firm bandaging from the middle over the location of the fracture with three or four layers, and as much as you move away from the site of the fracture with the bandage, your bandaging should be lighter, looser, and softer.
