And then extend both ends of the bandage towards the lower parts until it reaches the ankle, and secure both ends of the strips from the other side so that the bandaging is not moved from its place.
And then lay the bandage over that so it is such that no corrosion, abscess, swelling, or similar conditions occur to the limb; and if any of those causes should arise, then cut through the bandaging at that point and correct what has happened as soon as possible, as we have said before.
If there is in the bone a splinter of bone that must be removed, then you should cut it out as much as possible; and if not, cover it over and massage it, and treat with whatever preceded the memory until it is healed.
And in some cases, this fracture of the skull is strengthened by other things that are attached to it, as we have said about the helpers and the arm.
However, in the restoration of the limb that has been treated in this manner, lameness does not occur; but if it is restored alone, or if the other side is not similarly applied, then it is necessary for the person to limp permanently.
And you should know that the skull is strengthened in forty days or a few more days, and all of this depends on the diversity of constitutions and other circumstances.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of the fracture of the knuckle bone.
Know that the fracture of the knuckle bone often occurs at the joints, but it also happens in other places.
However, if there is a fracture, it is often merely a crack or bruise in its parts, and this is either a single injury or several injuries, and all of this depends on the sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that what is broken in its parts or mentioned should be cut out, and then joined and conjoined as much as possible by means of adjustment, ease, and fabrication.
And then apply a plaster and place over it a round plate if needed; and strengthen above according to the constriction as appropriate.
And therefore consider all the positions which we have recommended for other fractures, as well as abscesses and similar conditions, so that they may be applied in such a way that is suitable to every accident and appropriate to each case until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of a fractured thigh.
Know that in the thigh there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called the femur; the other is slender and is called the fibula.
And accidents of certain types of fracture occur to both bones in the thigh as do those to the two bones in the arm, and thus restoration is performed on it similarly to the restoration of the arm, and the operation for it is the same.
And if two bones are broken, the thigh is affected in all parts.
And if the more slender bone is broken, the thigh is affected in the forward parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is of those causes which are not within your control.
And then administer extension, reduction, and fixation of the fragments accordingly.
However, be careful if the thigh fracture is jagged with many fragments, that the extension be gentle and proceed with your skill.
And in the thigh there is some cause for operation similar to that of the arm, which is when you align the fragments and complete the entire procedure; at this time, you will take pieces of wood from a tree that grows below the causes that are stable on stones, and make them similarly from branches of palm trees; choose those with little curvature, and not those with fingers, and their length should be according to the length of the thigh, and tie them at both ends.
Wrap each cloth in two layers according to its length, and place one at the concave side of one part and the other at the convex side of the other part, with the seam directed towards the base of the foot.
Bind the two splints in three places at both ends and at the middle, for with this structure the arch is prevented from tilting to either the right or left side, and it remains in proper position.
And a wooden brace has already been applied according to the length of the arch; and place it in such a way that it preserves its range of motion.
And especially do this in the case of a fracture, and when it is properly a condition requiring such treatment.
And each day consider the arch and study it carefully based on previous observations, and keep it guarded from abscesses, inflammation, or other similar causes; and when any such cause occurs, then act according to what is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the ankle and of the bones of the foot and of those mentioned.
A complete fracture does not usually occur at the ankle; but fractures can happen to some of the bones in the foot. Fractures are less common for those bones, and they often suffer only contusions depending on their disposition.
And if the bones of the foot are fractured and those bones are elevated above their normal position, then the patient should place their foot on prepared ground as follows.
Then place something on top of that which is raised from those bones, and press down on them, and press, and bind them so that they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and place padding above it, and below the chest of the patient place a small table with two flat surfaces.
Then strengthen that limb with proper support, after you have wrapped it in cloths and with other means as you need.
And when three or four days have passed, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones bound, either broken or dislocated, and the treatment in such cases is as you know.
But if some say they are dislocated, then restore them, and bind them as I recommend to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place under the dislocated finger a stick according to the length of the finger, and add a little more; then below the patient place the table as I have recommended to you, and support it with proper binding.
But if what is dislocated involves two or three or more bones, then you place under each finger a stick of its appropriate size wrapped in linen cloth, and support the table at the lower part of the body, so that it stands on one of the flat surfaces of the table to keep the chest raised for proper retention.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes mentioned to you in other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and treat each case with what is appropriate.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the skull of a woman and of the bone of the penis and of the virile member of a man.
When the skull of a woman is fractured, at that moment make that incision in a crescent shape, and then slightly incline it towards the part of the back.
And then the midwife should fill the skull with cotton, and it should be placed in the skull as if it were a membrane; and then the woman should be bandaged, and elevate her back little by little, for all of this is done so that before the part of the skull that was fractured heals, the bone returns to its place.
And then place poultices on her back, and when she needs to urinate, remove the cotton to facilitate it, and after she has urinated, reduce and return it according to the method by which you prepared it at the beginning, and then return to the original poultice.
And do this for seven days or around that time, for it is then restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and stitch onto it a reed tube; then insert the entire bladder into the skull, and blow air through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the skull, for this will help the fracture to return.
And then fill it with cotton and keep it that way for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the penis in a man or woman is fractured, at that moment administer to its restoration and alignment what we have recommended for the hip bone.
In what is right, it is not hidden in these nearby fractures and few become aware of it, especially for one who has some study and practice and understands my book well; because that fracture means to you in the major dispositions through straightness in the restoration and constriction of it, and thus understands well.
When the penis is to be bandaged, take a piece of leather and place the penis in it; then wrap it with cloth coverings, and let it be wrapped for three days or around that time, and it will heal.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of bone fractures when there is inflammation.
It is appropriate for one who suffers a fracture with inflammation, and especially if the bone is large such as the femur or the humerus, or similar to these, that you proceed to incise and open it immediately if the conditions for incision are evident to you as we have said above.
And if the inflammation causes bleeding, then you should proceed to arrest it by dusting over it finely powdered sugar, unless another cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay it unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess develops, then you should postpone the restoration for nine days until the abscess subsides, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day, as you may cause further harm.
But if the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then you should proceed to reduce it and equalize it with your hands as easily and with minimal extension as possible.
If the extension is not prepared for you and the equation of it is too large, then at those times you reduce that to your own size with this tool; and this tool which is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight digits, and the width of which is according to the quantity of the wound; and therefore it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these according to what is needed for this in all kinds of fractures.
And let it be round, and let there be little grease on it so that it is not doubled over; during the operation, apply pressure on that.
And let it have a sharp tip at which there is a margin in its extremities; and let the upper part of it be thicker, and from the middle to the end be narrower.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "labrum voluntz atelarem," which means a small palette.
In order that the extremity of it may be steady, raise it up to the prominent part of the bone.
And press it in one motion so that the bone returns to its place, and let it be aligned with some support.
And thus strive to align the ends of the fracture between them, and if the broken end is delicate and does not take the tool well for some reason, then you should insert the end of that bone so that the instrument has control over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone entirely as we have recommended, then insert it with one of the probes that we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in some way that is possible for you.
Approach cautiously him who limps from roughness and small scratches in the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and during the reduction the patient feels intense pain in the tooth, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do what will greatly help the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then soak a cloth in black styptic wine, and if it is summer, apply it properly without pausing to cover the wound with wax or any other oily substance, so that decay and corruption do not occur.
And proceed to dress the wound at the appropriate time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you may excise any proud flesh with scissors and use bandages according to the extent of the wound.
And be careful not to touch the wound at the fracture site, for many times unskilled doctors have done this, causing death or incurable illness or severe harm to their patients. And if the swelling is slight, soft, and different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or is a bad wound, and you fear some of the harmful accidents we have mentioned, and you feel pain in the area, then it is not necessary to apply dressings; instead, make an enveloping bandage with hard cloths rather than dressings and apply it to that area.
And when after one day or two the wound begins to generate granulation, then you remove the cloth that you had placed over it with wine, and proceed to dress it with medications, as per our custom for treating wounds, such as tetrapharmacum and similar remedies.
And you should untie the bandage and examine the wound each morning and evening until it is consolidated and firm, and ensure that the member is prepared in such a way that the pus runs from it to the lower parts with ease.
If several days have passed and the wound has not consolidated nor has the flesh been separated from it, then you should know that this is due to the small veins, for which you must probe the wound with a probe. And of those veins that are separated and removed or extracted, those from which they cannot be separated and which cause pain when the member is moved, you should proceed to incise them as much as possible according to what is feasible for you.
And if the wound becomes infected with gangrene or erysipelas, which are severe forms of corruption and putrefaction, then you should proceed to one of those treatments as described in the preceding chapter on the treatment of such conditions.
And concerning the matter on which my discourse rests and you should present your approach: when the bone is broken, it protrudes from the limb as the bones do in the wrist and elbow and similar to those of large limbs, and you should not attempt to pull it out, nor its extraction after four days, for that might lead to death; instead, leave it inside where it can drain pus, for it will likely fall back into place within about twenty or thirty days; and then attend to the wound if visible, and if not, leave it be.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of swelling that occurs in the aftermath of some fracture.
Sometimes these nodes occur in the traces of healing of a fracture, especially near joints, and they deform the shape of the limb; thus you should prevent the limb from its natural movement.
Then consider whether the node is recent and administer medicines that have a styptic effect such as aloes, myrrh, cumin, saffron, and acorus; take any of these or all of them. Confirm with one of these styptics, egg white, or vinegar, and apply this over the node on lint, then bind it tightly with proper compression.
And let the bandage remain tight so that you do not loosen it for several days; then remove it and reapply another one until the node is softened; or apply a sheet of lead gently over it, because lead has the property of reducing anything swollen in limbs.
But if the node has already turned to bone and is hard, and the necessity requires that it be removed immediately, then cut into it at its most prominent part, remove the superfluous and prominent portion, or reduce it with some instruments until it is softened; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of fractures when they have been set and subsequently the limb is moved gently but not according to its usual nature.
When a bone fracture has been set and the limb is subsequently moved gently, this is done for various reasons: one of which is excessive soaking of the bandages and ligature not according to what is needed; or due to too much compression preventing proper nutrition from reaching the limb; or due to an excess of unnecessary poultices; or due to premature movement; or due to a lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
The treatment is to give the patient food, and make his heart tender so that blood may be multiplied within him; administer baths; and apply poultices and lint to him.
And apply plasters to the limb so that the plaster may draw nourishment to it, or continue rubbing with cold water, until nutrition flows and returns it to its natural form.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are set crookedly and it is prohibited to operate on them further.
When it happens to a limb that has already been restored near its healing from crookedness and prominence of the broken bone and nodosity, and it is because of this that the shape of the limb is considered flawed, but since the limb is not prohibited from its natural operation, then there is no need for you to accept the word of one who believes that member to be newly broken.
But there are false physicians and restorers who have done this in our land; and this operation is a reprehensible one leading to great fear, the sign of which is deadly.
But if limping and nodosity are recent, then it is necessary to rub them with cold water in which you soak softening herbs such as chamomile and radish leaves, and melilot, and similar ones; and a plaster should be made with a softening agent as well prepared; or take mucilage of radish seeds and mix it with flour of linseed or olive oil, and apply it as a plaster; or take fatty figs and grind them with dove's milk, and similarly with the medicines mentioned for strengthening consolidation.
And in some cases it is dissolved by constant gentle friction which is applied to the most fluid parts, and motion of the limb should be administered in all directions at all times.
If the inflammation has already advanced and become hard and converted into stone, thus necessitating treatment with iron, then it is necessary for the pus above to be drained and the continuation of the bone to be dissolved, and for that which is superfluous from the nodes of that matter on the bone to be excised with subtle instruments, and ease should be administered in this while being careful and studious.
Chapter XXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation.
Dislocation is the wrongful displacement of one of the joints from its proper place, thereby prohibiting movement, distorting the figure of the limb, and causing severe pain and great injury.
When a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to attempt reduction as soon as possible and not delay in doing so, for if it is delayed, inflammation will set in and make the reduction of the dislocation more difficult.
And for this reason, one should not wait or delay during the inflammatory stage, as doing so often leads to spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, it is necessary to prepare for the incision of the inflamed area, and then allow time for the inflammation to subside; afterwards, the limb should be bathed with hot water and oil, and then reduction should be attempted with ease.
And treat the entire limb with whatever remedies will come to mind for its proper care.
Here are the bones set in order as previously described for fractures; now I enter into the part concerning wounds more prevalent.
Chapter XXIIIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So when both jaws are dislocated and if they do not reduce easily; the dislocation of both can be done in one of two ways: either they are reduced from their positions with slight manipulation, and become slightly softened; or they are completely dislocated and thoroughly separated, such that they are softened at the joint where saliva flows from the mouth and the patient cannot close it, and the tongue is wrapped around the jaw.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its proper position on its own with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and thorough, it must be that reduction should be administered promptly, and not delayed at all.
And it is that the assistant hold the patient's head, and the physician insert his thumb on one side into the root of the dislocated jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side; or he inserts both thumbs simultaneously if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers are outside equally; and commands the patient to relax their jaws and move them from each side; and the physician equalizes the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated simultaneously, it must be that warm water and oil fumigation is administered, until gentle reduction can be performed on both, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And so when returning to the bandages, and the mouth of the wound is closed and not softened, then pause over those powders of cloth with wax, and let it be made of wax and rubbed oil; and afterwards let it be applied with ease to the soft parts.
And let the patient sleep on their side, and their head be placed between the two ears, so that it does not turn to either the right or left; and do not force them to chew for any reason; having their food should be cut up finely, until when the pains have subsided and the jaws are relaxed, then allow them to eat what they want.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and other actions, until the jaw is relaxed and healed.
And if the reduction of both jaws when they are dislocated at some point and not returned to their places is difficult, then fevers and frequent sweats occur, and the belly of the patient swells easily, and there is vomiting of pure bile.
And when you see this, then you know that it is hopeless; and many times the one to whom this happens dies within x days.
Chapter XXV.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the chest towards the interior by its continuation to the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this is perceived by sensation.
And for the restoration of the hand, the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then compress the area with your palm firmly, for it will return to its proper state.
And then apply the poultice and powder to that area.
But the extremity of that which is connected to the spine and continues from it is not dislocated on any day; therefore, it must be reduced and treated as we have said and according to what you have prepared.
And then apply the poultice, powder, and bandage, and instruct the patient to maintain tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this method of treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its proper place.
Chapter XXVI.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is when it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this happens rarely; and it is not dislocated behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in front by the nerves.
And it is most often dislocated and moved out at the lower parts below the joint, and more easily in those whose flesh is thin; but in those with thick flesh, it is harder to move out.
If it happens that any person is struck or falls and the muscle of the place becomes abscessed due to inflammation, and is rendered displaced as if it were dislocated.
Then proceed to treat it as if you were treating it with the methods appropriate for its condition, and now proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation of it is known when it is at the lower parts beyond the tendon; thus you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, for you find obvious differences among them; and you find a concavity at the head of the muscle, and below the tendon there is the touch of the head of the muscle as if by a man, and the injured person cannot return their hand to its socket, nor can they move that part.
If it is dislocated below the foot or above, then you immediately find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the practitioner raises the arm above the shoulder; then place your hands below the tendon, and with strength return the joint to its proper place, and the practitioner raises the arm, and extends it out straight; then let it rest as it returns easily.
But if it does not return as we have said, and after the initial days since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary to bathe the patient in warm water and administer a lotion that softens and relaxes as if for rheumatism, using substances such as mustard seeds, fenugreek, and mallow in water, and apply it.
Let the patient lie supine on their back and place under them a soft cushion of wool that is neither too loose nor too tight. Then the surgeon places his hand under the patient’s chin, elevates the neck muscle upward, and holds the patient's arm so it is not injured behind, while another assistant hold the patient's head so it is not hurt from behind, for that part immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: have another man present who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on the side where the injury is. Insert his muscle under the chin of the patient and elevate the chin upwards so that the patient hangs in the air, while another assistant holds the patient's arm from behind the patient’s stomach. But if the patient is heavier, it is necessary to find another way to suspend them, so that they do not strain themselves, for the dislocation will immediately return.
And it is reduced in this other way: Drive a long pole into the ground with its top rounded like a mortar head, neither too thick nor too thin. Place it under the patient; then lay strong cloths over the top of the pole. The patient should stand along the length of the pole, and extend one arm behind to the side opposite them. With strength, extend the body on the other side, as the joint will quickly return to its place.
And if the reduction of the patient is difficult due to all the causes we have mentioned, then use this treatment: Take a pole that is two cubits in length, four fingers in width, and whose thickness should be such that it fits under the chin according to this form:
And on the round head place linen cloths so that the flesh does not injure the patient.
And let a pad be placed under the buttocks of the patient, and let the arm or the hand be extended over the body between the parts below, and let the body be wrapped around the assistant and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
And let the arm be placed on a step of a ladder according to its length, and let the hand be extended toward the parts below, and let the other side be suspended from the other side, because the joint will soon follow.
And when the instrument is completed according to each manner that is possible for you, then place beneath the buttocks a pad of woolen cloth of equal thickness; and then place on an adhesive plaster prepared with powdered mallow roots and egg white over all the muscle, as mentioned above, and then tightly bind below the buttocks with proper constriction; and the bandage should be wrapped around the plaster, and the hand should be attached to the neck, and it should be left alone, and do not move the patient's hand for seven days.
And it is necessary to give the patient food in small amounts until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier for their recovery.
And then the food should be given more freely after seven or five days, and one movement should be exercised; and when it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated due to swelling that occurs or for some other reason, then it should be treated with cautery in three places, as was stated earlier in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after about seven days the bandage is removed and the joint has not been secured, and the plaster and strapping are reduced several times, and it cannot be fixed so that he can raise his arm above the part, then you know that the nerves which run to the end of the muscle have either been cut or stretched or weakened, and know that the joint will never be restored to its proper place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs from the foot and the hip or the posterior parts, the reduction is performed with impulse and extension as much as necessary until it returns.
And let the other strapping be administered there, and care for it until it is healed.
And if heaviness and slowness in movement occur near the healing of the limb, then the patient should use baths several times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the neck.
Know that the joint of the neck is dislocated with difficulty and seems to return with difficulty; and it can be dislocated in all directions, but mostly forward or backward. The dislocation is not concealed from you when it appears as if it has fallen away below the view or touch according to some figure it may have been displaced. And when you adjust the dislocated neck with the hand, this is clearly shown to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor reach its usual muscle.
And you should treat the neck, and reduce the dislocation as soon as it occurs before it becomes an abscess or ulcer, for if it reaches that state, reduction will be difficult, and full recovery is unlikely. This is especially true when the dislocation is to the rear, as this type of dislocation is worse than all others, more painful, and often leads to death.
And the restoration of it is such that when it can be reduced, the hand should be extended across two boards, and the arm should be expanded, and the physician's hands should be on either side of the joint below it, and he should press the joint with his fingers together or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
If the dislocation is to the front parts, then it returns in such a way that the hand is bent towards itself, until it strikes the elbow with the palm of his hand.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then vigorous extension and strong motion should be administered.
And it is that two ministers extend the arm, and there should be those firmly holding both sides so that it does not move due to the extension.
And the arm should be rotated around completely, as it is wrapped in folded cloth long over the hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that these are those causes which help in the lubricity of the joint for ease; and then press the joint with vigorous pressure until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary for him to rest on a plaster that has styptic and cohesive properties with an egg albumen base, and be bandaged tightly, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and he should be left alone for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and thus if the joint is formed in its proper place, now you loosen the bandage of the arm and let it be.
And if you see that the joint should not be stitched, then apply the plaster and bandage again, and leave it like that for several days until it is healed; after which you may remove it.
If it happens that there is great pain during the reduction of the joint and its position is in motion, then administer moistening with baths and gentle friction, and compression until it becomes calm; or place upon the joint something cold, which reduces movement; and afterwards bind it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And afterwards take that off and place it in water; when it is cool, then you should rub the joint with moderate friction, and afterwards apply something cold to it again, which reduces movement, once, and the second time, and the third time, with a bathing instrument until it is finished.
And if you wish, place on it pieces of boiled and softened cowhide and stitch them; and do this several times, for these are moist and help to restore its position.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow joint, which is between the arm and the hand.
Dislocation of the hand sometimes occurs, and the reduction of such dislocation is easy, but the opposite is true for other joints. However, it is important to act with speed in reducing it at the hour when it has occurred, before swelling sets in or an abscess forms.
And the reduction of such dislocation is that you place the joint of the hand of the patient on a table and the assistant extends the patient's arm, and the physician places his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint, and presses it until it returns to its proper place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is fixed to the belly of the hand. If this is the case, then the patient should place the back of their hand on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation precedes the fingers, then the position of the hand should be placed directly on the table so that the doctor's hand can act immediately upon the joint.
And if it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then strengthen that area with a bandage over the abscess and leave it like that; and do not repeat this procedure, because otherwise, it will neither be endured nor can you prevent the pus from spreading over the days.
However, the joint moves according to its curvature; and if you notice nothing in the patient that would cause this, except that the hand is soft and cannot be strengthened by any means; then know that the nerves are compressed or torn, and there is no remedy but to cauterize it properly for the case and strengthen it as far as possible without causing further injury.
When it has been returned properly, then place the bandage that we have recommended on it, and leave it and strengthen it for five days; and afterward, remove it and leave it, and exercise the hand.
However, if the movement of the limb is restricted and you encounter any severity in it, then apply it with warm water and friction several times until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
On the Treatment of the Dislocation of the Said Joints.
In some cases, the said joints are dislocated completely.
And when a finger is dislocated at one of the joints toward the palm or toward the base, hold the finger steady and apply pressure to return the dislocation with your fingers until it returns.
And then hold the tip of the finger, and suspend that part which is dislocated, and leave it like this for two days.
And then suspend that part and extend it until it is properly bandaged on that day.
And when it is night, wrap that part according to the narration, and do not stop unwrapping it during the day and exercising with movement, and wrap it at night; and do this for several days until it is strong.
And do the same for this if it is dislocated at the base of the hand, and wrap it in that part and perform the first operation like this until it is healed.
And do the same for this when it is dislocated at the other parts.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the back or neck and the vertebrae move out of their places, then there is no cure in that case, for death is feared for the patient.
The sign of this is that the excretion from the patient occurs without their will, and they cannot control it; and often some parts of them become weak or two limbs or two arms or one of these; or a vertebra is displaced from its place, as sometimes it moves and sometimes the displacement goes to four directions.
Thus it is turned toward the rear parts and is named gelbozitation.
The cure for it is that you consider whether the gelbozitation occurs due to puerperium, because at that time there is no cure and no healing from it.
But in the case where it comes from trauma or impact or something similar to those, the Ancients described the methods of treatment at length, much of which returns to being overly detailed; but I have abbreviated that part, sparing from excess due to my closeness to understanding and my exposition, with which I have made this explanation and formation from a different instrument than that with which I would show this and demonstrate that.
And those are not cured except for the one that occurs precisely as I mention.
And it is that the patient lies on their back near a wall, and is placed below a well-padded cushion so that the legs are not strained.
And nearby a straight post should be placed, fixed in the ground at the end near the head of the cushion, and another post around the feet at the other end of the cushion.
And the minister should hold one post, and it should not be fixed in a hole; another minister should hold the other according to that form.
And nearby wrap around the head of the patient and below the shoulders with a firm linen bandage; extend the end of the bandage to the post that is behind the head, and secure it there; then tighten the other bandage over the hips near the armpits, and behind the thighs; then adjust all the bandages and secure them to the other post that is behind the feet.
And the minister shall extend the ligature at the part of the stump, and let not the two stumps and their respective ligatures be removed from the places in which they are fixed, but rather let them not be secured as we have said.
And the physician shall place his palms on the strong part of the spine until it returns, or let him place upon that one table, and then adjust upon the table his own weight until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, now take a table whose width or length is about three arms, and dig into the wall near the patient a place where the end of the table can fit. And then place half of the table on the spine, and the physician place his weight on the other end, and gently pull until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper place.
And if you wish, open again the one that is revolved with the hand; and it is that you place on the ground behind the patient's head at the end of two stumps, the length of which is one arm, and behind the patient's neck at the end of two similar stumps; and let the distance between each stump be a palm, and each should be firmly fixed to the ground so that it does not move.
And there should be one round rod in which is the wheel, and it should revolve in the two sockets of the two stumps.
And at the end of it is a socket in which the rod is formed, the length of which is one palm where it revolves; and on the other two stumps there is something similar to that.
Unbind the bandages that you have tightly applied to the patient’s feet at the time when you were preparing to cut off his head, and the bandages that you have bound tightly around his thighs at the time when you were supposed to cut off his legs.
You should have each turn held by a minister with his hand, and the surgeon should twist the spine as we have said.
And after the spine is returned to its place and the area has been adjusted, now you should apply an excrescent plaster on a piece of cloth, and then add cotton; and afterward place over the plaster strips of wood with a width of three fingers or thereabouts, and a length that covers the affected area around the spine.
And then wrap it with the bandage as we have described, and use a light diet for the patient until he is healed.
And if there is any stiffness in the area at the end of the healing process, you should administer medicines that soften and lubricate over an extended period according to our recommendation; and a sheet of lead has already been applied to that area.
And at times stiffness occurs in the lower part of the spine due to dislocation; and that bone is such that it may become dangerous if you attempt to treat it with this medicine, as it could easily lead to death.
Chapter XXXI.
On the Treatment of a Dislocated Hip.
Know that the joint of the broad part and the joint of the muscle is subject only to dislocation, and does not suffer what happens to other joints with small movement and concavity.
And the joint of the broad part is dislocated in four ways, for it can be dislocated inwardly, outwardly, forward, or backward.
And it is most commonly dislocated inwardly, and rarely forward or backward.
And the sign of dislocation inward is that when you adjust the leg of the patient on the healthy side along with the affected side, it becomes longer and the heel more prominent than in a healthy state, and the patient cannot bend their foot towards the shin, and this place where bending occurs is prone to abscess from cauterization, so the head of the bone has already reached here.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs outwardly is that the symptoms are contrary to those of the aforementioned condition.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs forward is that when the leg is raised according to its full extent, it does not bend due to pain in the heel, and if they attempt to walk, they cannot move forward; and their urine is retained and their swallowing is difficult, and they should walk on the heel.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs backward is that they cannot extend the heel, nor can they bend forward as much as when swallowing, and the leg is shorter than the other, and swallowing is difficult, and the head of the bone is in that location.
The final reduction of the kinds of this dislocation is that you consider, at this time, if the dislocation is old and ancient, which already has been there for a long time, you administer reduction to it and rest according to its disposition; now it is not in that cure entirely; and then it does not have to be made with excessive force.
In the case where the dislocation is recent and is one of the four kinds of dislocations, now you should look to that which will return the joint to its proper place, extending it both inward and outward, and move it from side to side, for it returns easily without needing further treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, now you should prepare a strong assistant and extend the limb from below, or with its fingers, or with a bandage that lies above the joint; and another assistant extends the hand of that person above so as to place their fingers below the joints; and then tighten a lion's skin bandage over the crook of the arm, and have another assistant hold tightly at the end of the bandage.
And this extension of the limb or the part inward to the angle of the shoulder blade, or of the part behind to the part of the back.
And this extension of all these at once should be done while the patient is raised off the ground and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is common to all four types.
And so if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, now it is necessary for all kinds what I will say about its proper treatment.
In the reduction of the shoulder, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is to the inner side, the patient should be laid on his back; and then place the bandages along the ridge of the shoulder in that part which lies between the head of the humerus and the clavicle; and then extend the bandage from the angle as far as possible towards the sternum.
And then another strong assistant should take hold of the patient’s arms and forcefully press down on the thick part of the dislocated humerus while extending it outward with great force, for thereby it returns to its place.
And this particular case is the easiest to cure among those in which this member is reduced compared to the other cases.
But if it is excused on you and you do not obey the exercise with this particular method of treatment at all, then bind both sides of the patient tightly together with a strong, light bandage over both heels and both toes.
And let the length of each of them be four fingers, and let the affected side be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended at the top of a post and held by his hands; and let the distance from him to the ground be two arms' lengths.
And then command a strong youth to press down on the head of the humerus, and let another youth hold the patient, while this other youth presses the arm with great force, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the reduction of the shoulder, when the dislocation is to the outer side, the patient should lie on his back as we have recommended for treating stiffness; and let the bandage be tightly wrapped around the affected side and over his feet.
Place the poles beside him, one at his feet and the other at his head; and place beside them rods adjusted to the middle of his back, laid upon which wet cloths should be wrapped around him, so that the patient is not harmed by the pole being among things belonging to him, so that the extension to the parts below is not pulled away.
And each minister extends to his part, and the surgeon with his hands applies the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then it is necessary that the bench be extended quickly, and according to this method of narration on the back; and the surgeon places the palm of his right hand on the patient's lower abdomen, and with that compression uses the other hand to extend towards the rear parts.
And if the dislocation is to the rear parts, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended towards the back, and he should be raised off the ground; but it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary in the same way if his hip is dislocated outward, according to what we have said about positioning him on two boards; and it is on his face with his limbs extended, as we have said, otherwise there will be no cure.
And it is necessary to administer compression on the table at the location where the joint is.
Thus, when the reduction is complete as it should be — and the sign of the reduction is not hidden from you, and that is when you extend the patient’s limb in two directions, and when you see those lines and the patient moves the affected limb and expands it for its own comfort, then you know that the member as it should be has been returned — and then you adjust two things and pause with bandaging and wrap both bindings such that the width is not diminished in any of the parts; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or four.
And then loosen the bandage and wrapping, and compare one limb to the other.
Now, if you see those lines clearly, then you know that the dislocation has already been fixed, and then allow the patient to go.
And if you see any sign of inflammation, then you return and treat that area, and bandage it according to your initial structure, and allow it to rest for three days.
And then loosen that area, and allow the patient to delay in walking on it for several days until it is comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated outward, inward, and downward towards the back; it cannot be dislocated forward.
And the sign of elbow dislocation is that when you instruct the patient to apply their limb to the joint, then you know that the elbow is dislocated.
And for the restoration of all methods of which you must perform surgery on the patient rightly, namely that you extend his leg if possible; adhere your hand to his face and place your foot between his thighs; then proceed to adhere the tip of his penis with your palms, and hold those between your said hands firmly upon the tip of his penis so that it returns to its place.
And the sign of recovery for him is that his leg adheres to the body without laborious effort.
And then apply a plaster to that and make his leg adhere to the body, and then bind those together with a bandage for three days or four, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days until he is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of it by you is unnecessary, now administer extensive treatment with the bandages as previously described in the cure of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the thigh.
The thigh sometimes is displaced by slight movement, and sometimes it is dislocated completely, and the dislocation of it occurs either toward the interior parts or toward the exterior parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the thigh swollen proceeding toward the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its displacement is easy recovery, and it should be extended with ease using bandages so that it returns to its proper place.
In the treatment of the humerus when it is dislocated as a second step, make sure you lay the patient straight, and let there be a strong and capable minister behind him at his mid location.
And place yourself with your right hand on the most superior part of the foot, and with your left hand on the lower part of the foot at the location of the heel.
And then pull the foot towards your right hand, and then place your left hand underneath twice, and then extend the left hand; and press the foot's sole with your right hand against the thigh while working.
And do this two times as we have counted.
And then press the sole of the foot against the thigh for the third time, and you will be at the heel.
Therefore, if it returns in one attempt or two according to this method, and if the foot becomes straightened, it is good; and if not, then proceed with the operation again, as it has returned.
But if it is prohibited for you to reduce it as we have recommended, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and place a sturdy board fixed to the ground under them, and let it fit between the bones, and you should wrap the patient in a cloth so that they do not move.
And then the minister holding the patient should hold their bones, and another minister should extend the foot with his hands or with a bandage placed on the neck of the foot; and then each minister should stretch against the extension of their side, and the board should be extended between the patient's bones retaining it, so that the body does not be pulled towards the parts below during the extension.
The surgeon holds the displaced bone with his hands, and another assistant holds the healthy bone in place below, for the displacement returns easily.
And when the displacement returns and the reduction is declared to be healthy, apply a plaster and wrap it tightly with strong bandages, and bind the limb to the chest.
And be careful to avoid the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over them is not too tight and does not injure them severely.
And let that be for two days or three; and if the bandage is loosened, wrap it again.
And on the third day or fourth, remove that bandage, and prohibit the patient from moving for forty days. But if the patient is forced to move before that time, there is no guarantee that the displacement will not dissolve and become corrupted, and then it will be beyond cure.
And if an abscess needs to be cauterized, then you should proceed according to the instructions in our narrative on that subject, in another section about treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Said Ankle Bones.
Ensure that the person who is dislocated is treated with some extension without labor; and what is not difficult is often easy.
But if the dislocation is in any of the joints of the foot, then you should have the patient lie down with their affected foot on a firm surface or table, and position it as if they were standing.
Stand aside and let the patient lie on what is below the joints, and then press with your foot against it firmly until it returns to its place, so that you see it flattened and does not appear in a prominent location.
Then place the sole of the foot on a table that will support the entire foot, which has two ends, and then bind it with a proper and firm bandage for three days; then unbind it and let it rest by walking on it for ten days, until it is strengthened and recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
About the types of dislocation that are caused by injury, fracture, or any combination thereof.
When any of those causes occur, and you proceed with the treatment of it and the restoration of the patient, sometimes death may result.
And because of this, one should not presume to undertake the treatment themselves, but rather it should be done by someone who is wise in the art, experienced over time, compassionate and pious, neither hasty nor presumptuous; and at the beginning, use medicines that only reduce swelling, and leave the patient's fate to divine judgment, unless there is hope of recovery from the illness and where some promise appears. And then strive to perform the reduction for the patient as soon as possible at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes swollen and severe.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, immediately administer a regimen that reduces swelling and treat the injury with what is appropriate from among the dry remedies.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and it happens in the bones that are separated, then you perforate their membrane, and do what we have said about the simple diseases concerning what precedes in those places.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that which pertains to the instruments in the ways of fear as it has been foretold in my preaching; for this is better for you to serve your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more salutary for your true body, which will be revealed in His time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly; may God be blessed for them. Amen.
